Project development objective Re$ PAD B.2, Technical Annex 3

The objective o f the Project i s to support the Government's program to provide basic education
and improve the quality o f education for all children by 2010: (i) ensuring equity o f access to
basic education; (ii) enhancing quality and learning achievement; (iii)  providing opportunities for
furthereducation andtraining; and(iv) strengthening education sector management.

These objectives are all framedwithin the context o f the Government's Economic Recovery
Strategy and its governance strengthening and anti-comption program.

Project description Re$ PAD B.4, TechnicalAnnex 6

The Program consists o f 23 investment programs, which are listedbelow and grouped by area o f
improvement.

 1. Ensuring equity of access to basic education. Primary school infrastructure; non-formal
schools; special needs education; adult basic education; expanding education opportunities in
arid and semi-arid lands; gender and education investment program.

2. Enhancing quality and learning achievement. Early childhood and development; school
health, nutrition and feeding; primary school instructional materials; pre-service teacher
education; in-service teacher education; quality assurance and standards; in-service training o f
teachers at secondary inmath and science; information and communication technology in

                                                ..
                                                11

education.

3. Providing opportunitiesfor further education and training. Guidance and counseling;
secondary education; TIVET anduniversity education.

4. Strengthening education sector management.HIVJAIDS;capacitybuilding; education
management information system; teacher management; monitoring and evaluation; information
dissemination; and accountability -all within the framework o f the sector's strategy for good
governance-with a specific focus on secondary andpost secondary sectors.

Inallofthe investmentprograms, emphasis isplacedoninformation dissemination,
transparency, accountability and addressing corruption, within the context o f the sector's strategy
for good governance, and with an increasing focus on secondary and post-secondary sectors.

Which safeguard policies are triggered, if any? Re$ PAD D.6, TechnicalAnnex 14
Environmental assessment and involuntary resettlement safeguard policies are triggered by the
project.

A. STRATEGIC CONTEXT AND RATIONALE

1. Country andSector Issues

Economic Development and theAnti-Corruption Program

1,      Kenyais currently goingthrough an importanteconomic transitionperiod.
The country's weak economic performance duringthe 1990s, coupledwith apopulation
growth rate averaging 2.7 percent, ledto a contraction inreal per capita income by an
average o f 0.5 percent annually inthat period. The current Government, elected inlate-
2002, has moved forward to address economic management issues andto strengthen
poverty reduction efforts. Upon arriving inoffice, it prepared an Economic Recovery
Strategy for Wealth and Employment Creation (ERS), reached agreement with the
International Monetary Fund(IMF) on a Poverty Reduction and GrowthFacility (PRGF)
program, moved to improve relationships with donors, anddevelopednew strategies for
economic and social development. Progress on the macroeconomic agenda has been
encouraging, with a doubling inthe growth rate from 3 to 6 percent from 2003 to 2005.
Despite severe drought conditions, year 2006 growth may even exceed last year's level.
There has been a strong effort to improve government revenue mobilization, with
domestic revenues outstripping more modest increases inexternal grants. Government
expenditures have risen more slowly, due to more rigorous financial management
controls and a slower than expected recovery inpublic sector investment, resulting from
various implementationbottlenecks. Consequently, the Government has beenable to
reduce rapidly its budget deficit and dependence on domestic borrowing. This has helped
lower domestic public debt, and sharplyreduced both nominal andreal interest rates.
The resulting more favorable domestic borrowing conditions, accompanied by rising
business confidence have ledto an encouraging revival indomestic investment.
Inflation, as measured by the consumer price index, has remained highreflecting both the
buoyancy inthe private sector and the knock-on effects o f imported inflation due to rising
energy prices andrelated increases intransport costs.

2.      AlthoughPresidentialelections are fast approachingbyDecember2007, the
Government's commitmentto sound economic managementso far seems robust.
For example, on June 15,2006 the new Minister o f Finance gave the annual budget
speech and it presented a sound pro-poor, business friendly and growth-oriented budget.
Expenditure and revenue aggregates are broadly inline with the commitments made
under the Government's program with the IMF. Inparticular, further progress has been
made inrealigning the pattern o f government spendingto the priorities laid out inits
ERS, withnotable increases inspending onroads, education and especially rural health,
where Kenya's long-term track recordhas beenparticularly dismal. The latest budget,
builds on earlier efforts to shift expenditures toward pro-poor spending, but with
increased emphasis on women, children andyouth, and a welcome initiative to curb
expenditure inlow priority areas, including the perks o f Government officials.

3.      Havingcampaignedon an anti-corruptionplatform, the new Government
beganto putinplacea legal andinstitutionalframework to advancethis agenda.
For example, Parliament passed the Anti-Corruption and Economic Crimes Act and the


                                               1

Public Officers Ethics Act in2003; the Public Audit Act (January 2004); the Government
Financial Management Act (January 2005); and then the Public Procurement and
Disposal Act (October 2005). The Government launched an enquiry into a large
corruption case (Goldenberg), dismissed top judges, suspended procurement officials
associated with corruption, created the Kenya Anti-Corruption Commission, and
compiled an Anti-Corruption Action Plan focusing on hrther actions to be taken in
2005/06.

4.      However, by mid-2006,there was a clear consensusamongKenyansand
other stakeholdersthat the Government's effortshadnot met expectations. InJune
2006, a World Bank missionworked with the Government to review progress on
governance strengthening. This mission concluded that, while some improvements had
occurred inthe period 2003-04, results were stagnating and reversals were occurring in
important areas like thejudiciary and procurement (though apparently not to pre-2003 so
far). Overall, many stakeholders believed that the whole anti-corruption campaign
needed to be re-energized around a clear and more limited set o f priorities, with a strong
emphasis on prompt and decisive implementation, and on systematic tracking and the
measurement o fresults (see Annex 1). Itwas also generally recognizedthat progress had
been uneven between sectors. Specifically, inthe education sector, as noted below, it
was recognizedthat considerable achievements had been made instrengthening
accountability inthe primary sub sector. More details on the conclusions o f the
governance mission are included inAnnex 1(paragraph 27). Inaddition, the Detailed
ImplementationReview (DIR) carried out inthe first halfo f 2006 concluded that the
fiduciary safeguards inthe education sector were reasonable (paragraph 15).

Access, Quality and Government Spending in the Education Sector

5.      Progresshasbeenmadetowards universalprimaryeducation,particularly
since the introductionofFree PrimaryEducation. Kenya is now making considerable
progress inreversingthe erosion o f education gains made since the first two decades o f
independence, and instrengthening the governance o fthe sector. Immediately following
its election, the new Government announced its commitment to Universal Primary
Education (WE) as its top priority, and introduced its Free PrimaryEducationpolicy
(FPE) inJanuary 2003, which included abolition o fprimaryschool fees and levies
charged to parents. As a result, an additional 1.2 million children, who hadpreviously
dropped out or never attended school before, increased total enrollment inthe 8-year
primary school programto 7.5 million (7.2 million inpublic schools and 0.3 millionin
other schools) by year 2004. The primary school net enrolment rate (NER) continues to
increase, with data for 2005 showing an additional 250,000 children inthe 18,500
primary schools, following the massive increase o f enrollments inthe previous year. The
NER(including private andnon-fonnal schools) wasjust 80 percent inyear 2003, andit
is now about 83 percent on average, although there are still slight disparities between
boys and girls (the NER for boys i s 84 percent) and especially betweenthe regions.
Progress i s also beingmade towards having all children complete primary schooling by




                                            2

2015. The present primary school completion rate (PCR) i s now about 80 percent', and
the school drop-out rate is now coming down, partlydue to the improved learning
environment inschools where the availability o f textbooks has increased substantially.
Significant changes are also beingmade inthe curriculum for both primaryand
secondary education, with the number o fprimary subjects already reduced andwith the
introduction o fnew primary school textbooks.

6.       The introduction of FPE was accompaniedby significant measures to
increase social accountability and improve governance at the primary level. These
included the direct and efficient disbursements o f funds to primaryschools through the
commercial banking system; the strong involvement of local communities inthe
innovative instructionalmaterialsprogram; strengthenedmonitoring and accounting
systems; improved transparency and dissemination o f information; a comprehensive
consultation process with a wide range o f stakeholders; and enhanced accountability,
with more disciplinary actions taken against wrong-doers. The effectiveness o f such
measures inthe primary sub sector has beendocumented by several independent reviews
and audits. For example, an independent public expenditure tracking survey had already
been completed in2005, concluding that "Overall, the flow o f funds has been efficient,
with schools receiving funds allocated on time. Bottlenecks encountered inthe flow are
being addressedby MOE. At school level, funds receivedhave been correctly recorded
andused for intendedpurposes. A large majority o fthe schools haveput inplace
systems that ensure transparency and value for money". As noted below, comparable
actions are now also being extended to the post primary sub sectors o f education; and
programs inother sectors (for example, inthe arid lands program, inthe HIV/AIDS
community-based activities, and more recently inthe health sector) are also now adopting
similar systems o f governance.

7.       Challenges remain at the primary education level. However, to remain on
track toward the educationMillenniumDevelopment Goal (MDG), the Government must
confront huge challenges even at the primary education level. For example, even though
many schools, especially inurbanareas, have experienced a massive influx o f children,
there are still nearlyone million school-aged children who are out o f school. Some o f
these children are inthe urban slums, some inthe Arid and Semi-AridLands (ASAL)
areas (for example, only about one quarter o f children o fprimary school age attend
school inNorth Eastern Province), and some inpockets of poverty elsewhere. School
buildingsthroughout the country are generally ina state o fpoor repair, and there are not
enough classrooms or water and sanitary facilities for the increasingnumbers o f children
now coming to school. Besides access, there are also significant quality challenges in
primary education. The results from the Southern and Eastern Africa Consortium for
Monitoring o f Educational Quality (SACMEQ) study show that Kenya is doing better
than most ofthe other 14 SACMEQ participating countries, but performance inabsolute
terms i s low. For example, less than 24 percent o f students in 1998 achieved the


1   The primary completion rate inKenya, with the duration of primary schooling at 8 years, i s the number
    o f children enrolled ingrade 8 divided by the number o f 14 year old children inthe population. The
    PCR is expected to rise because enrollments inthe lower grades have increased partly due to the
    enrollment o f over-age children since free primary education was declared inearly 2003.


                                                     3

desirable level o freading, and there is considerable variation inperformance largely
determined bypoverty and rural location. The distribution o f teachers i s also a major
related issue. From a national perspective, the overall number o f teachers is not a major
constraint, eventhough the pupil-teacher ratio has risen from 31:1(end o f 1990s) to 43: 1
(in2005). However, there are shortages ofteachers insome schools, withteachersnot
deployed on a fully equitable basis. Some progress has beenmade recently through a
teacher "balancing" exercise, and the Ministryof Education (MOE) andthe Teachers
Service Commission (TSC) have developed new staffing norms to guide the appointment
o fteachers. These norms need to be formally approved and implemented. There i s also a
needto further strengthen education management at all levels andto implement proposed
decentralization plans. Schools are already accountable for funds provided to them for
the purchase o fbooks and stationery, andthey also manage funds for development
activities; but there i s need for further capacity buildingat the local level, including for
the implementationof the new school infrastructure program now beingpiloted.

8.      There are significant pressureson secondaryeducation, due to increasing
numbersof primaryschoolleavers and affordability. At the secondary level, there
are also some major issues. Inrecent years, the transition rate for children from primary
schools to public secondary schools (of which there are about 4,000) has been inthe
range o f 44-49 percent, and it i s now 51percent. The gross enrollment rate (GER) at the
secondary level i s about 30 percent, and 79 percent o f those who enter secondary school
complete all four years o f secondary education. However, the increased number o fpupils
at the primary level i s puttinggreat pressure on the limited secondary school places
available, even though costs are highfor parents. The development of secondary schools
inthe past has essentially been a community responsibility, andthe increasedpoverty
level inthe country particularly inrecent years has hampered their establishment. The
Government i s now aiming for the transition rate to the secondary level (including the
existing 500 non Government secondary schools) to increase to 70 percent duringthe
KESSPperiod.

9.       Reformsinstudent access, teacher deploymentand schoolfees are neededto
addresssecondary educationpressures. Work is currently underway on the
development o fthe strategy for the expansion o f secondary education, and especially on
the respective roles to beplayedbythe Government, local communities andthe private
sector. The key issues relate to access to secondary school places, appropriateness o f
secondary education for school leavers and labor force requirements, and ability o f
parents to pay for school fees. The existing plans include an expansion o f existing
secondary schools to an average of three streams, the establishment o fnew (day only)
secondary schools especially indeficit areas, andthe refurbishment o f existingsecondary
schools. There will also be a reform of the existing bursary scheme to benefitthe poorest
children, andchanges made inteacher deployment to increase the low studenuteacher
ratio. However, further work i s needed to ensure that the key issues are addressed ina
comprehensive strategy for the sub sector.

 10.     An institutionaland governancestrategy is also neededfor Technical,
Industrial,VocationalandEntrepreneurshipTraining(TIVET). This is an



                                                4

important sub sector o f the education system, with training institutions runmainly by the
MOST andthe Ministryo fYouth. Private organizations, individuals, NGOs and
religious organizations also runTIVET programs inabout 1,500 training institutions. In
addition, about 600 youth polytechnics are meant to provide an avenue for absorbing the
increasingnumbers o fprimary and secondary school leavers. A national skills audit
studyhas recently beencompletedandthe results show that: (i) is abigdemandfor
                                                                    there
generic skills, such as personalcharacter, inter-personal skills and ICT skills; (ii)
specialized technical and vocational skills are also needed; (iii) although Kenya has many
trained personnel, many are unemployed and the quality o f their skills i s relatively low;
and (iv) there i s a widening gap between technologies found inindustry and those
provided duringtraining. As a result, and inresponse to the situation inthe sub sector,
there is now a consensus that there is particular needto: create an autonomous body to
co-ordinate all the institutions within TIVET; revise the curricula inthe various
institutions, inconjunction with the private sector andindustry; rehabilitate manyo f the
institutions and upgrade some of them; institute quality assurancemechanisms at the
institutional and national level; and include TIVET inthe loan scheme o fthe Higher
Education Loans Board. Along with these plans, there i s need to strengthen the
governance o fthis sub sector too. The Government i s committed to developing a
detailed strategy for the TIVET sub sector, including with enhanced accountability and
increased dissemination o f information, prior to making significant investments init,

 11.     In highereducation,there are also major issues of access, governanceand
financingto be addressed. Kenyahas 6 public universities and 17private universities,
with a student population o f approximately 90,000. Enrolments have risenrapidly with
the increase inthe number o fpublic andprivate universities, andwith the introduction o f
privately sponsored students inpublic universities. This trend is likely to continue to put
pressure on the ability o f universities to deliver quality education, and staff morale has
 generally beenlow due to significant resource constraints. However, recent
 improvements interms and conditions o f service, combined with increased finances from
 student fees, have had some positive effects. There have also been some welcome
 developments inuniversity management, for example with the appointment o f
 Chancellors (other than the President himself) to the universities; the transparent and
 competitive selection of Vice-Chancellors; the establishment o f a Public Universities
 InspectionBoard (PUIB); and the reform o f the HigherEducationLoans Board (HELB).

 12.     These developmentsare promising,but further policyreformsin
 governance,managementandquality assuranceare now requiredin higher
 education. These include measures to: definemore clearly the desiredsize and shape o f
 the whole university system, including public, private and international providers; ensure
 that universities adhere to their core mandate o fteaching and research; and reform higher
 education financing. There i s also a need to strengthen and harmonizethe legal
 instruments and roles o f the different bodies currently involved inuniversity governance,
 andto augment the ongoing efforts to improve universityadministration, management
 and autonomy. These measures include: defining the degree o f Government involvement
 inthe management o fpublic universities(buildinguponthe recentlycompletedreport o f
 the PUIB); clarifying the respective roles o fthe Commission for Higher Education (CHE)
 andthe UniversityCouncils; takinginto account the recently drafted CHE strategy;


                                                5

recruiting university managers competitively; and increasing student and staff
representation at universities. As inthe case o f TIVET, the Government is committed to
developing a detailed strategy for the university sub sector prior to makingsignificant
investments init.

13.     Governmentexpenditureinthe educationsector is substantial,at about 7
percent o f GDP. The total annual Government budget for the education sector i s
presently (FY06/07) about K Sh 99 billion2, or roughly US$1.3 billion. Education
spending as apercentage o ftotal Government spendinghas rangedbetween 25-30
percent over the last five years, with recurrent expenditureas a proportion o f total
Government recurrent expenditure beingas high as 36 percent in2004/05, but with
development spending fluctuating inthe range 1-8 percent o f total Government
development expenditures inthe last few years. The annual development expenditures
for the education program inKenya (including both Government and external support)
through the budget are inthe range o f K Sh 5-7 billion (US$70-90 million). As part o f
the total education budget, there are certain items identified as core poverty reduction
activities. These include activities such as school feeding, the school health and de-
worming programs, special activities for schooling inthe ASAL areas, and secondary
school bursaries for poor children. The budget for core poverty programs ineducation i s
about 16 percent o f the total educationbudget. The core program is reviewed and
assessedannually as part o f the Ministerial Public Expenditure Review (MPER) process.
Projections show an upwardtrend inthe proportion allocated to these programs (to about
19percent inFY07/08).

14.     Cost of teachersconstitutesthe major share ofpublicspending. Withinthe
education sector, primary education accounts for 54 percent o f the total, and secondary
education accounts for 26 percent of the total. Oftotal public spending on education,
about 63 percent i s on the cost o f teachers. Within the primary sub sector, teachers'
salaries account for about 86 percent o f the total. Without adequate funding for non-
salary expenditures, it is likely that the school-children, newly enrolled since the
introduction o f free primary education, will not receive a quality education and that the
level o f drop-outs inthe school system will start to increase again. Innovative policies
andstrategies are neededto reduce unit costs, addressthe heavy cost burdens on
households, andimprove the internal efficiency o f the schooling system by reducing
repetition and drop-out rates, and by making better use o fteachers.

Governance in the Education Sector

 15.    Governanceinthe primaryeducationsub sector is generallyencouraging.
As discussed above, the introductiono fFPEwas accompaniedby significant measures to
increase social accountability and improve governance inthe education sector, especially
at the primary level. The recent Detailed ImplementationReview bythe World Bank's
Integrity Department encouragingly found         ". ...a satisfactory level o f accountability in
the FPEprogram, and a reasonable extent o f fiduciary safeguards for the sector as a


2   This excludes K Sh 800 million for secondary school bursaries, presentlyallocatedthrough the
    Community Development Fund(CDF).


                                                  6

whole". Within the country, governance inthe education sector is, indeed, generally
recognizedas beingstronger than inother sectors inKenya. For instance, over half o fthe
respondents inthe National Corruption Survey o f the Kenya Anti-Corruption
Commission (KACC) believed that corruption levels had improved over the last year in
education offices (77 percent) and inpublic schools (75 percent). This heartening picture
o f governance i s due to several factors, including committed and very competent
leadership (despite the resignation o f the Minister o f Education inFebruary 2006
following corruption allegations); greater knowledge anduse o fthe Code o f Conduct
among officials; a series o ftraining activities for a large number o f staff on fiduciary and
anti-corruption issues; the proactive communication and stakeholder consultations
strategy of the MOE; comprehensive and strong monitoring and auditing systems (e.g.
including introduction o f a ministerial audit committee and a riskbased interimaudit
function inDecember 2005); documented guidelines andprocedures for implementation,
andkeypartnerships, such as that with commercialbanks for more efficient transfer o f
resources to primary schools and with communities at the school level to promote
monitoring and accountability.

16.     However, even at the primary level, there are further governance actions
required, including those identifiedby the recent DIR for the FPESP. These measures
include an additional review o f disbursements to schools under the FPE program, as some
disbursementschedules prepared by the Textbook ManagementUnit included a few
schools without bank account numbers, causing accounting and bank reconciliation
difficulties. Also, despite an agreement that bank charges would not be levied on school
FPE accounts, it appears that a few schools have had to pay such charges; insuch cases,
the charges will be repaid. Measures identifiedto strengthen the Kenya EducationSector
Support Program framework include the need to retain an independent internationally
recognized agent to monitor the procurement process, establish an integrated financial
management and procurement database, simplify MOE accounting tools andprocedures
at the school level (under FPESP andKESSP), and tailor the type and frequency o f
training sessions on Bank procurement to MOE. A fbrther assessment on the
implementationof the free primary education program including identification o f any
outstanding governance actions, will beundertaken inFY07 as part o f the
Implementation Completion Report (ICR) for the FPESP that is due to close inDecember
2006. Preparation o f the report will involve strong stakeholder participation and
fiduciary expert participation, including emphasis on fiduciary issues. When produced,
this report, andparticularly the lessons o f experience on governance, will be publicly
disseminated,

 17.     The sector is already raising further the bar on governance and anti-
corruption. Notwithstanding the progress already made ingovernance particularly inthe
primary education sub sector, the Government recognizes that there i s still unfinished
business and absolutely no room for complacency. Inthis regard, MOE developed a
governance strengthening action plan inJuly 2005 and recently updated it inAugust
2006. This sector governance planembraces six key themes: (i)       monitoring and
evaluation for results based management; (ii)    community involvement and social
accountability; (iii)consultations, transparency and information disclosure; (iv)



                                                7

transparent and efficient resource allocation; (v) fiduciary management and controls; and
(vi) organization, management structures, accountability and legal framework. In
addition to the actionplan, the Ministry o f Finance (MOF) has also written a side-letter
emphasizing its commitment to increased disclosure o f information inthe education
sector, a strengthened public complaints systems, intensifieduse ofrisk based internal
audit, continuing capacity buildingon fiduciary issues, reform o f the TSC, and the
extension o f governance strengtheningmeasures to secondary and higher level education
facilities. On disclosure o f information, the action plan features innovative ideas such as
publicizing information on disciplinary actions, fundingallocations, procurements,
selection o fprimary school leavers to Form Iinsecondary schools, teacher selection and
other management decisions through the web, public notice boards andmedia. The
improved complaints systementails hotlines established at headquarters andboxes at the
district level. The action plan also takes forward the inclusion o f education issues inthe
national governance survey scheduled for October 2006 (see paragraph 20 for more
details), the development of an integratedfinancial and procurement system database,
and the plannedrecruitment of an independent agent to monitor procurement activities
(as recommended by the DIR). M O E has already put inplace strengthened oversight
structures (e.g. inter-ministerial, steering and senior management committees and
consultative forums with the private sector and development partners) and intends to
enhance social monitoring (e.g. through community score cards, capacity building, and
information on financial, quality assurance, procurement and instructionalmaterial audits
or reviews, including Bank supervisionreports on performance). The July 2005 and
August 2006 versions o f the action plan and the side letter are to be found inAnnex 2.

 18.      Special attentionneededto governanceissues inthe Teacher Service
Commission. One particularly important area o fgovernance strengthening concerns the
TSC, especially following a critical report on it by Transparency International. This
report hadbeenexplicitly requested by the TSC management, and it identifiedbriberyin
teacher recruitment. Since then, field agents have beenappointed with delegated
functions; and an Appeals Tribunal has been established at the national level to
 adjudicate on recruitment issues; professionalcommittees have been set up to deal with
 any misconduct cases involving employees; a code o f ethics has beendeveloped for
employees; management has been decentralized to the district and school levels; and
recruitment guidelines havebeen reviewed to minimize the danger o f corruption. Under
the new Action Plan(Annex 2), there is now commitment to: develop andimplement a
more detailed plan for the prevention o f corruption; further sensitization o f all TSC
 structures and staff on corruption issues; ensure that the newprocedures for teacher
recruitment are working effectively; conduct a comprehensive audit and review o f the
 TSC Staffing Department; work with KACC to revamp the TSC IntegrityDivision; and
 carry out enhancedpublicity campaigns especially on teachers' governance issues,
through monthly magazines andposters. TSC senior officials are already subject to
wealth declarations, and the TSC i s committed to having continued independent
 monitoring o fthe progress beingmade in strengtheningthe transparency and efficiency
 o f teacher hiring.




                                               8

19.      Particulareffortswill be made in governancestrengtheningat the secondary
andpost secondary levels. Comparable actions to those taken at the primarylevel are
now being extended to the post primary sub sectors o f education. The key elements o f
the future strategy will include extension o f efforts to increase the dissemination o f
information at the secondary and tertiary levels; evenmore accountability to the public;
continuous capacity buildingandindependentmonitoring o f activities. For example, at
the secondary level, the measures will include improved guidelines for the management
o fresources; capacity buildinginfinancial management andprocurement; public
expenditure tracking; the posting o fnational examination results andForm Iintake
already on the website; revisingthe code of ethics for teachers and enforcement
mechanisms; and extending social accountability (with increased community
involvement) and the use o frisk based internal audit. As inthe primary sub sector and as
noted above, steps will also be taken to improve the selection andutilization o f secondary
school teachers through reforms inthe operations o f the TSC. Procedures will be
strengthened for the transparent recruitment o f secondary teachers, the restructuring o f
the TSC Integrity Department (withthe Headbeing selected competitively from outside
the TSC) and the training o f officials ingovernance and anti-corruption. Similarly, for
the Exams Council, there will be a review of the management o f examinations and
enhanced information about examination results. This will require continued, sustained
efforts by all officials and the leadership o f the MOE. There will be a major review o f
the findings o fthe recommendations on governance strengthening inthe PUIBreport.
This wouldprovide an excellent platform for a discussion o foptions for moving forward,
including through the possibility o f additionaljoint analytical work andthe development
o f a new Higher Education Strategy, ifneeded.

20.       Addressingand monitoringthe governanceissueswill extendbeyondthe
MOE. Inparticular, the implementation strategy willbe assistedbyactivities undertwo
IDA-supported projects. First, the Institutional Capacity BuildingProject (ICBP), which
i s designed to strengthen the management o fpublic expenditure and improve budget
preparationand execution, reporting and evaluationprocesses. With the support o fthe
project, Government i s also deepening the focus on results as an integral part o f good
governance, and is makingprogress inrolling out the results-based management system
inthepublic sector, includinginthe education sector. Second, the Statistical Capacity
BuildingProject (STATCAP), whichwill strengthen monitoring activities bythe
Government (including the forthcoming governance survey3), and support public
disclosure o f information (including through performance contracts). The monitoring o f
the governance strengthening activities for education will be done largely through the
Joint Reviews, which take place every six months, but will also take advantage o f
reviews o f the results of periodic surveys, program evaluations and new data availability.




3    The Government, in collaboration with several development partners and stakeholders, i s preparing to
     carry out a nationwide assessment from October 2006 to measure how corruption and other issues
     related to public sector performance ingovernance are perceived by the public.


                                                    9

2. Rationalefor World BankInvolvement

21.     The proposededucationoperationsupportsthe Bank's CountryAssistance
Strategy (CAS) andthe ERS. The latest CAS, completed inMay 2004, isresults-based,
drawing on the framework o f the ERS. It aims to help Kenya achieve its development
objectives through four areas o f support: (a) strengthening public sector management and
accountability, parastatal reform and privatization, andmonitoring and evaluation
capacity; (b) reducing the cost o f doing business and improving the investment climate;
(c) reducing vulnerability and strengthening communities, including support for increased
agricultural productivity, improved environmental management, strengthenedlocal
governments, and reducingpoverty inthe poorest rural areas andurbanslums; and(d)
investing inpeople. Table 1shows the work program for the education sector from the
CAS, insupport o f the ERS. The CAS progress report, which i s under preparationand
will be distributed to the Boardduringthe second quarter o fFY06/07, emphasizes
continued support for educationbut with increased focus on governance strengthening,
comprehensive sectoral reform especially at the secondary andtertiary levels, anda
continued harmonized approach with other development partners.

22.     IDA providessubstantialexperienceandlinkswith other nationalreforms.
IDAis one o fthe sector's largest external financiers (together with the Department for
International Development (DFID)),has supported the education sector over many years,
and i s very familiar with it (though some o f the knowledge now needs to be updated,
especially inthe TIVET and higher education sectors). The World Bank has substantial
experience with large-scale education reform programs, and it i s able to identify best
practices inother countries with similar development challenges to inform the reform
process inKenya. The proposedoperation i s a natural follow-up o f the IDA-supported
FPESP. Although the education projects supported by IDA inthe 1990's had




                                              10

unsatisfactory ratings intheir respective ICRs4,the ongoing FPESP has had consistently
highsupervision ratings (endorsed by arecent review bythe Quality Assurance Group).
The primaryreason for this isjudged to be the clear commitment demonstrated by the
Government towards the FEPprogram and more generally the strengthening and reform
o f the education sector. The World Bank i s also well positionedto strengthenthe link
between education reform, macro-economic stability and the Medium Term Expenditure
Framework (MTEF), inaddition to the development o f other sectors such as agriculture,
health, water, infrastructure, legal, and social protection.

23.      The consolidation of governance strengthening in primary education and its
extension to the secondary and post secondary sub sectors are another rationale for
the involvement of the World Bank inthe education sector. While the overall
conclusion o f the DIR for the FPESP andthe KESSP was favorable, further work i s
clearly needed, as noted earlier. This i s considered to be an area o f comparative
advantage for the World Bank, giventhe opportunity to buildon our experience inthe
sector and our increased attention to governance issues and anti-corruption issues at the
country, sectoral and transaction levels. These efforts would be supported by the Bank in
increased public dissemination o f information, including supervisionreports on the
project's performance. There i s a newjoint assistance strategy now under compilation,
and it is expected that the World Bank will continue to support the education sector in
collaboration with other partners, but with increased focus on the post-primary sub
sectors and on governance issues.

24.      Other external agencies have also been major supporters of the sector, but
have recognized and welcomed Bank leadership, as noted later inthe discussion o f
partnerships. For example, the assistanceo fDFIDdatesback to the endo fthe 1990s
whenthere was little external assistanceto the sector. The DFIDeducation support
program (Strengthening o f Primary Education: SPRED 3) provided financial support for
the purchase o f learning materials for needy primary school pupils and for in-service
training o fteachers. Recently, DFIDhas announced additional support amounting to
GBP55 million (about US$lOO million) for the KESSP. Together, IDA and DFID are the
largest external agencies inthe education sector. However, there are also other partners
supporting the sector. These include the Governments o fBelgium, Canada, Germany,
Italy, Japan, the Netherlands andthe United States, as well as UNESCO, UNICEF, the
European Union, the Aga Khan Foundation, the African Development Bank and OPEC.
International NGOs, such as Action Aid and OXFAM, are also supporting relevant
policy-related studies. Kenya i s also one o f the countries benefitingfrom the expanded
EFAFast Track Initiative, with approved fundingfrom the Fast Track Initiative Catalytic
Fundamounting to US$24.2 millionper annum for CY05, CY06, and CY07. The funds
for CY05 have already been disbursed. Some support provided by development partners
i s provided through pooled funding, while some support i s provided by other
development partners working through project mode within the KESSP framework.




4    EducationSector Adjustment Credit(1991), University InvestmentProject(1991) andEarly
     ChildhoodDevelopmentProject(1997).


                                                11

3. HigherLevelObjectivesto which the ProgramContributes

25.     The Governmenthas recognizedthe strategicimportanceof raisingthe
overall educationlevel of Kenyanswithinthe contextof povertyreductionand
economicgrowth, Within the ERS, education is seennot only as a welfare indicator, but
also as a key determinant o f earnings and, therefore, an important exit route from
poverty. As a result, human capital investments, including health and education, are
crucial to the achievement o f the ERS objectives. The comprehensive, results-based,
logical framework o fthe ERS also shows how the priority programs will contribute to the
achievement o f specific targets, including the MDGs and the Education For All (EFA)
goals. These specifically include the achievement o fUPE, raising the primary GER and
NER(to 100percent), and closingthe gender gap inaccess to primary andsecondary
education.

B. PROGRAMDESCRIPTION

1. ProgramConcept

26.      The concept buildson achievements, consultationsand agreedpolicy
frameworks. Buildingonthe achievements o fthe FPEprogram, the Governmenthas
developed a sector-wide program, the KESSP, covering the five Government ofKenya
(GoK) fiscal years beginningJuly 1,2005, and ending June 30,2010. The planning
process has included a process of consultations with a wide range o f stakeholders (with a
national EducationForum at the end o f 2003); a series o fJoint Review Missions, with
about 20 external agencies participating, starting inSeptember 20045; the development o f
an Education Sessional Paper, "A Policy Framework for Education, Training and
Research", approved by Parliament inApril 2005; andthe KESSP document itself. The
program concept i s also framed within the context o f the Government's governance
strengthening and anti-comption program. Inparallel with the development o fthe
program concept, hrther reforms took place inthe education sector: the Ministryo f
Education Science and Technology (MOEST) was reorganized, the MPERprocess was
strengthened, the annual consultations arrangements were institutionalized, and a review
was launched o f the legal framework for education, training and research.

2. ProgramDevelopmentObjectivesandKey Indicators

27.      The goal of the KESSPis to providebasic educationandimprovethe quality
of educationto all childrenby 2010. There are four program objectives o fthe KESSP
including: (i)   ensuringequity o faccess to basic education; (ii)    enhancing quality and
learning achievement; (iii)  providing opportunities for further education and training; and
(iv) strengthening education sector management. These objectives are all framedwithin
the context o f the Government's Economic Recovery Strategy and its governance

    full year o fKESSP implementation.



                                                12

strengthening and anti-corruptionprogram. The key Development Objectives indicators
are shown inTable 2 below. Inline with best practice, the key indicators contained inthe
legal agreement for the project have been limitedto only 4, relating to the NER, PCR,
learningachievements andtransition o f students from primaryto secondary schools6. A
largerResults Framework, which also includes Intermediate Outcome indicators, is
shown inAnnex 5. Further indicators for the post-primary sub sectors will be agreed as
part of the development o frespective strategies for them. The Program consists o f 23
investment programs, and IDA support would be used to finance eligible investment
programs and expenditure items, buildingupon the successes o fthe past and lessons
learned, and with an increasing focus on the secondary andpost-secondary sectors.

29.      The Governmentis also fully committedto improvinggovernanceinthe
educationsector andis adoptingclear policiesto do so. A s noted earlier inparagraphs
15-20,the progress o f governance strengthening inthe education sector is generally
encouraging already, but the Government intends to raise further the bar on anti-
corruption efforts and activities. At the primarylevel, there are h r t h e r actions required,
althoughthe mainfocus will be on enhancing governance at the secondary andpost
secondary levels. Addressing the governance issues will extend throughout the
Government, although the lead will b e taken by the MOE.



                                                16

30.      The Government's policiesfor strengthenedgovernanceand accountability
inthe educationsector are set out inthree key documents (see Annex 2). First,the
Minister o fEducationcommittedthe Governmentto a Governance andAccountability
Action Plan inJuly 2005, when the KESSP was launched. The strategy focused on the
following areas: (a) community involvement; (b) resource allocation; (c) consultation and
social accountability; (d) teacher management; (e) public expenditure management; (f)
procurement; and (g) financial management. The planshowed commitments for the
fbture towards a more transparent and accountable environment inthe education sector,
inadditionto documentingimportant progress already made. Second, inAugust 2006,
the MOEreviewed andthen updatedits governance strengthening plans producing a
reorganized Action Plan structured around six key themes (listed above inparagraph 17).
This plan included several innovative features, particularly on disclosure o finformation
and efforts to address corruption at the TSC (see paragraph 18). Third, the latest action
planmatrix is accompanied by a side letter from the MOFwhich underscores
Government's commitment to deepening governance initiatives (specific initiatives are
provided above inparagraph 17). The education sector governance plans are also
summarized inthe Government's overall governance action plan o f July 2006.

31.      The Governmentis fully committedto monitoringthe progressof these
governancestrengtheningmeasures, andjoint reviews are underway. As indicated
earlier inparagraph 20, there is a process o f six-monthly joint consultative reviews. The
firstjoint review o f the KESSP implementationtook place inOctober 2005; inMarch
2006, there was the first joint budget review workshop for the KESSP, according to
schedule; and the first review o f KESSP implementationi s underway inOctober 2006.
The monitoring process also takes advantage o f special reviews o fthe results o fperiodic
surveys, program evaluations andnew data availability. Inaddition, monitoring on
governance strengthening for the education sector will feature prominently inthe nation-
wide annual governance survey andinthe public sector results-based management
initiative, both described above inparagraph 20.


4. ProgramComponents

32.      KESSPis structuredaroundfour areas of improvementcovering23
investmentprograms. KESSP's 23 investment programs are listedbelow, including
their costs as estimated inthe KESS ProgramDocument o f July 2005, and a brief
description o fprogram activities (see Annex 6 for more detailed descriptions, and Annex
 7 on costs). The programs below are grouped by area o f improvement.

 33.     Area 1: EnsuringEquityin access to basic education. The investment
programs to achieve this objective are aimed at reducing disparities ineducation
 opportunity due to wealth, geographical location and gender, by creating an opportunity
 for all children (including those o fthe poorest families and those livinginremote areas)
to attend a school o f acceptable quality. The investment programs include the following:

37.    The 23 investmentprogramswould beimplementedwithin the framework of
the KESSPand activitiesto enhancethe strengtheningof governance. While the
investment programs would be fundedandmanaged separately, for accounting purposes,
they would be carried out inline with the Government's commitment to enhancing
governance strengthening and combating corruption. They would also be fully integrated
into the KESSP and its monitoring. Duringthe appraisal mission inmid-2005, the
development partners collectively emphasized their commitment to the Sector Wide



                                             21

Approach Program (SWAP), andreaffirmed their support for the KESSP inits entirety.
However, it was agreed that the KESSP investment programs will have a particular focus
on girls, orphans and vulnerable children, inorder to enhance the extent to which they are
pro-poor. It was also agreed that a phased approach should be adapted to the
implementation o f the KESSP with support from external partners. Initially, the focus
will be on the basic education sub sector, including pre-primary, primary and adult basic
education; support for the EducationManagement Information System (EMIS),
monitoring and evaluation (M&E), and capacity development, including strategic studies
o f other sub sectors; and cross-cutting issues (quality assurance, gender, HIV/AIDS,
guidance and counseling). Incontrast, it was agreed that secondary education, TIVET
anduniversityeducation will beconsidered for pooledfinancing underthe KESSP, only
after agreed strategies for these sub sectors hadbeenproduced. It was also agreed that
the use o f the pooled funding support would be limitedto specifically agreed line items,
corresponding to the agreed list o f eligible investment programs. The initial list o f
eligible line items i s included as an attachment to the Joint Financing Agreement (Annex
11).


5. Summaryof ProgramCosts andFinancing

38.     InJuly 2005, the recurrentand `investment' costs of KESSPwere estimated
at KSh 543 billion(US$7.2 billion)over five years (seeTable 4). The total investment
cost o fKESSP over the period was estimated at US$1.4 billion (see Annex 7 for more
details). However, levels o f "investment program" expenditure for FY05/06 and
FY06/07 are now foreseen to be somewhat lower than originally anticipated, consistent
with actual andexpected external aidreceipts for these two fiscal years. Implementation
o fthe KESSP inFY05/06 focused particularly on the primarysub sector, with
continuation o f the successful instructionalmaterials program andplanning for the school
infrastructure development program. Fundingwas primarily from the Government's own
resources, though major support of about US$lOO million was approved by DFID. The
implementationexperience o f the first year o f the KESSP will bejointly reviewed in
October 2006.

39.     The currentestimatefor the five-year externalfinancingrequirementis
US$744 million,ofwhich US$SO millionwouldbeprovidedby the proposedIDA
Credit. Substantial commitments havebeenmadebyDFID(US$lOO million), andby
the Fast Track Initiative (FTI) Catalytic Fund(US$73 million, for disbursement during
the first three years). Other education sector development partners include the African
Development Bank (ADB), EuropeanUnion (EU), OPEC, UNESCO, UNICEF, WFP,
and the Governments o f Belgium, Canada (CIDA), Germany, Italy, Japan (JICA),
Netherlands and the USA (USAID). Intotal, o f the external financingrequirement o f
US$744 million, two thirds (US$467 million) has been secured. It i s expected the
commitment o f the IDA Credit will encourage other partnersto contribute to the program
andreduce the financing gap, o fUS$277 million (4 percent ofthe total program cost).
Some o f the additional development partners are expected to participate in supporting the
education sector through the pooled fund. Ifthe financing gap i s not closed, however,

thenadjustments wouldbemadeto the expenditure programminginthe secondhalf of
the KESSPperiod.

6. IDA LendingInstrument

40.     The proposedIDA instrumentto supportthe KESSPwill be a four-year
Sector InvestmentCredit (SIC) ofUS$80 millionequivalent. The expected date of
effectiveness of the IDA credit i s January 1,2007, the program completion date i s
June 30, 2010, and the Credit closing date i s December 31,2010.

41.     The proposedsupportfromIDA will beprovidedwithinthe frameworkand
principlesof a SWAp, througha pooledfundingarrangement. As noted earlier
(paragraph 37), pooled funding support would be limitedto specifically agreed line items
within an agreed list of eligible investment programs. Consultations betweenthe MOE
andthe development partners contributing to the pooled find wouldtake place at the
annual review ineach October and especially at the annual Joint Budget Review
Workshop to be heldineach MarcWApril, o f the KESSP duration. At the latter meetings,
the pooling development partners would state the funds that they intendto provide to the

                                                    23

Ministry of Educationto support KESSP inthe coming financial year. The Ministry o f
Educationwould thenmake a proposal to the poolingdevelopment partners as to the
prioritization, process and proportion o f funds to be allocated across the eligible
investmentprograms. Inline with FY05/06 where KESSP fundingwas focused mostly at
the primarylevel, it is planned for pooled fundingto be usedmostly for primary
education investments inFY06/07. The fundingo f the post-primary sub sectors will be
dependent on the development o f satisfactory strategies for them. To the extent that other
external resources become available to close the financing gap from FY07/08 onwards,
the available IDA funds are then expected to beusedincreasinglyfor the post-primary
levels.

42.      The disbursementswill be made against actual, incurredeligible
expenditures,basedon quarterly FinancialMonitoringReports(FMRs). The MOE
opened a pooled KESSP account inJanuary 2006, and received FTIsupport into it via a
Special Account. The MOE has successfully prepared a quarterly KESSP FMR for the
quarter April-June 2006. The maximumthat could be withdrawn from the IDA Credit
account will be US$20 million equivalent, each fiscal year. There will also be allowance
for up to 10 percent o f the IDA credit to be usedto cover retroactive expenditures
incurredwith effect from July 1,2006.

7. LessonsLearnedandReflectedinthe Design

43.      The design o f KESSP reflects important lessons that have beenlearned from
experience with SWAps inthe social sectors inthe Africa Region, from international
experience with the implementationo f education reforms, and from earlier externally-
supported education projects inKenya.

44.      SWAps can contributeto the increasedeffectivenessof aid andhelp
governmentsto implementsector reforms, as shown inthe experience inthe Africa
region - for example, inthe Uganda Education and Ghana Health sectors. But these
benefits may not fully materialize unless key donors agree to harmonize requirementsfor:
(i) financial management andprocurement (aligning them with those o f the Government);
and (ii) progress reporting and implementationreviews. Budget support, pooled funding
andjoint missions (for preparation, appraisal andreviews) are all instruments that are
being used elsewhere for this purpose with considerable success. To support the KESSP,
key donors have already begunto work together and are readyto use these instruments.

45.      Thereis considerableinternationalexperiencein developedanddeveloping
countrieswith the design andimplementationof educationreformandquality
improvementprograms. The balance o fthis evidence suggests four important lessons.
First, ultimately the success i s determined at the school and classroom byteachers, local
administrators and community members working together and acquiring new skills.
Strengthening the capacity to manage change at this level is, therefore, a key ingredient
o f the implementation design. Second, improvement usually requires a comprehensive
approach that involves improvement inthe availability of key inputs- competent
teachers, an adequate supply o f instructionalmaterials, well organized curricula and
enoughtime for learning - as well as improvement inthe way inputs are usedfor


                                              24

learning. Third, an effective system o f teacher support and continuous in-service learning
i s thus a keyingredient o f a successful reform process. Finally, educationreform and
quality improvement is a process that has results that buildup gradually over a period o f
years, and that requires the commitment and involvement o f many stakeholders.
Consultations, open andtransparent discussions with stakeholders, and a willingness to
consider lessons of experience and alternative approaches are all an important part o fthe
process. The experience with education projects confirms the importance o f these
lessons inthe Kenyan context.

46.      The capacitybuilding, quality assurance, andin-servicetrainingactivities
includedinKESSP are designedto providetechnicalsupport to parents,community
members,teachersand administratorsat all levels inthe system. The FPESPand
SPRED experience has demonstrated the effectiveness o ftransferring financial resources
directly to schools and decentralizing the procurement o f instructional materials to that
level. KESSP builds on this experience by continuing the school level procurement o f
instructionalmaterials, and extends it through the provision o f grants for infrastructure
development to SMCs and BOGSand the provision o f grants to ECD committees. To
ensure continuous learning from experience, it provides for a systematic process o f
monitoring and evaluation and progress reporting.

47.      Recentexperiencein the educationsector is encouragingbut also pointsto
the needfor sustainedcommitmentsto governancestrengthening. Keyelements
include strong mechanisms for social accountability, maximumtransparency and
disseminationo f information, independent monitoring and wide involvement o f
stakeholders inprogram design and supervision. The DIR for the FPESP also confirms
the importance of sustained capacitybuildingtogether with systems development (such
as integration o fprocurement and financial management data). All o f these lessons of
experience have beenincorporated into the design o f the KESSP.


8. AlternativesConsideredandReasonsfor Rejection

48.      In consideringthe appropriatedesignfor supportingthe KESSP, various
options were consideredbesidesthe proposedSector InvestmentCredit. One option,
instead o f an SIC would have been a Development Policy credit with untied budget
support. However, it was felt that that option will not presentlybe appropriate for the
education sector inKenya as: (i) are still some significant concerns about capacity
                                   there
for governance and financial management inthe sector; and (ii)    the M O E and MOST still
need some intensive assistance and capacity buildingincluding inthe design o f specific
programs. Onthe other hand, it was also felt important to move forward from a
"conventional" approach, inwhich disbursements will bejust for discrete works, goods
andservices on a transactional basis. The instructional materials component o fthe
FPESP has already shown that a broader approach could be adopted successfully, and
that there is need to move to a broader program-based approach which i s more efficient
and suitable for scaling up investments inthe sector. The largelypositive experience
withboth SPRED andthe FPESP inbuildingcapacity for local management and
accountability, including resource transfers via the MOE, have also encouraged


                                              25

development partners to move away from the direct control o fprojects, increasing
Government ownership.

49.     The possibilityof an AdaptableProgramLoan(APL) as the lending
instrumentwas also considered. Through consultations, however, a decisionwas
reached that a single four-year Credit, with disbursement conditions for some sub sectors
for which strategies are being developed, will be the preferred lending instrument for
supporting the SWAP.


C. IMPLEMENTATION

1. PartnershipArrangements

50.     The MOE leadsthe donor coordinationprocess, with the Permanent
Secretary,MOE chairingthe meetings with the developmentpartnerson a
quarterlybasis. Additionally, there are two key, substantial meetings each year -the
Annual Review inOctober, andthe budget planningmeeting inApril. On the
development partner side, the EducationDonors' Coordination Group (EDCG) i s chaired
on a rotational basis, with frequent meetings on both a regular and as-neededbasis. The
agreed Partnership Principles (signed bymost development partners inNovember 2005)
for the Kenya education sector are presented inAnnex 12.

5 1.    The proposedsupportfor the KESSP by the developmentpartnersbuilds
uponthe very successfulexperienceofjoint work. Inparticular the work between
DFID, CIDA, SIDA and the World Bank insupportingthe FPE Program, through with
instructionalmaterials and school-based teacher development. Thus far, DFID, UNICEF
and IDAhave committed to support KESSP through Joint Financing Agreement (Annex
11) signed onDecember 16,2005, for pooledfunding. It is expected that some more
development partners will support the KESSP with pooled fbnding, while others will
provide earmarked support within the KESSP framework.

2. InstitutionalandImplementationArrangements

52.     A comprehensiveKESSPCoordination,ImplementationandAccountability
Structurehasbeendeveloped. MOEST was restructured in2004 and was then split
into the M O E and the MOST" at the end o f 2005. An Education Secretary was
appointed competitively, along with five Directors o f Education (one o fwhom
subsequently transferred to the MOST). These arrangements are mainstreamed,
integrated into the regular work into the MOE, andutilize existing structures (such as
DEBS).These arrangements are valid and appropriate despite the creationofthe separate
MOST (see Annex 8 for details).



10 The MOST is responsible for the Directorate ofTechnical, Industrial, Vocational and Entrepreneurship
    Training, the National Council o f Science and Technology, the Technical Training Institutes, and
    Researchand Development. Responsibility for universities remains with MOE.


                                                    26

53.     The success of the FPE programowes muchto the highlycommitted
leadershipof the MOE. Mucho fthe responsibility for the implementationo fthe
reforms over the last three years has rested on a relatively small number o fkey officials.
Together with some signs o f fatigue, there i s also some concernrelating to the imminent
retirement of several senior managers. It will be crucial for the Government to ensure
that the achievements o f recent years are not undermined, but rather are carried forward
through sustained efforts.

54.     There are some new systems that havebeen designedto strengthen
implementation,coordination,andinternaland externalaccountability. Internal
coordinationhas been greatly enhanced under the revised institutional structure with the
formal establishment o f the KESSP Steering Committee and the KESSP Reform
Secretariat. The Reform Secretariat consists o f a core team o f education experts
supported by the Team Leaders responsible for the 23 KESSP investmentprograms.
Each o fthe 23 KESSP investment programs are being coordinated and managedby line
staff inthe MOE and MOST. The implementation o f the KESSP i s based on frontline
structures and staff, particularly the District Education Officers (DEOs) and school
management committees. The program will, as much as possible, rely on mainstream
Government financial management systems. It will buildon existing systems relied upon
duringimplementationo fthe ongoing IDA-financed FPESP (see more below, and in
Annexes, on financial management andprocurement).

55.     The KESSP institutionalstructurehas a numberof accountability
mechanismsthroughwhich externaleducationstakeholderswill holdtheir
counterpartsdirectlyengagedin service provisionto account (see Annex 8 for
details) These are designed to support the ongoing efforts o f the Government to
overcome an entrenched culture o f corruption inmany areas o f service provision. This i s
most critical at the frontline o f service provision, where district education boards (DEBS)
hold (DEOs) to account, andparent-teacher associations (PTAs) holdtheir school
management committees (SMCs) to account. At the school level, best-practice formal
institutional accountability mechanisms will be introduced, such as the report card
concept developed elsewhere, to enable service users (parents and students) to assess and
report on the annual performance o f their school inthe delivery o f education services.
Usewill also be made o f the findings o fthe recently conducted client satisfaction survey
(which showed generally very positive views towards the FPEachievements).

56.     As already indicated,key annualevents will includethe JointReview in
October, andthe budgetplanningmeetinginApril. The final annual work program
andbudget for each fiscal year will havetaken into account the views andcomments o f
IDAand other development partners

3. MonitoringandEvaluationof Outcomes/Results

57.     The monitoringandevaluationof the KESSPwill be of criticalimportance.
The MOE education management information system (EMIS) will be used to monitor
key education indicators. The M O E already has reasonable capacity to monitor key
indicators, such as the NER and PCR (with estimates for 2005 already available). A new


                                                27

school census questionnaire has been designedand piloted inlate 2005. However, the
EMIS systemstill urgently needs rationalization(addressing duplications, introducing
electronic formats, coordination o f data collection and entry), data needs to be shared
better withinthe MOE, between the Ministry andthe TSC, andwith the outside
community. There is also a need to improve the monitoring o f governance inthe sector,
andabaseline surveywill be carried out inSeptember 2006. The results ofthe Kenya
IntegratedBudget Household Survey o f 2005 will also be utilized for monitoring and
results based management. Inaddition, there will be coordination o f the various national
learningassessmentinitiatives: (i) National Baseline Surveys on Learning
                                   the
Achievement (supported by the FPESP); (ii)     the Student Achievement Monitoring
Investment Program (SAMP); and (iii)    the National Assessment System Investment
Program, so they couldjointly be used as a monitoring tool. MOEwill periodically
report on actual public expenditures inthe sector. Although it may not bepossible to
evaluate the impact o f every intervention inthe KESSP, a limitednumber o f
interventionswill be chosen for thorough impact evaluations (Annex 5). The MOEwill
report comprehensivelyon progress made inachieving the program targets at the annual
Joint Review and budget workshops to be heldinOctober andApril respectively. The
management of the information collection and disseminationprocess will be the KESSP
Secretariat.


4. Sustainability

58.     The KESSP goals includea primarycompletionrate of close to 100percent
inprimaryeducation,and a transitionrateof 70 percentfromprimaryto post
primaryeducationby the endof 2010. The numbers of students enrolledinsecondary
education are expected to increase rapidly, whereas those inprimaryeducation will rise
slowly due to a decline inthe growth rate o f primary education student population
(following the rapid expansion with the introduction o fFPE). Such an increase instudent
enrollments will require substantial additional resources for the education sector. Ifthe
share o fpre-tertiary educationintotal Government spending is kept constant, simulations
show that the available public resources will be adequate to cover projected expenditures
over the planperiod o f the KESSP, to 2010 (Annex 13). Bythe end o f the KESSP
period, the primary education sector could attain a completion rate o f 100percent on a
sustainable basis, and also make significant progress inreaching other FTItargets by
2015.

59.     The secondary educationgoal of 70 percenttransition rate, on the other
hand,is only sustainableunder a stringentset of policiesto be implementedwithin
the nexttwo years. The sustainability o fthe education sector duringthe reform period is
crucially dependent on limitingthe total number o f teachers. Especially as the extent of
pupilrepetitionhas decreasedrecently, only relatively small efficiency improvement
could be obtained by further reductions inrepetition. However, further gains in
efficiency couldbe obtained by increasingthe work hours o f secondary teachers. The
simulations indicate that increasingthe number o f teachers who teach several subjects
will bringabout the largest gains inefficiency, and sharplyreduce the deficit in
secondary education. However, there is needfor the non-Government sector to play the


                                              28

major role inthe expansion o f secondary education to assure the financial sustainability
o fthe pre-universityeducation system. Such additional support from non-Government
sources will be critical to meeting the growing demand. The economic analysis suggests
that the followingpolicies are needed to reachthe goals o f the education sector program
ina fiscally sustainable way:

    (i)themanagementofteachersshouldbeimprovedbydeployingtheminprimary
         schools on the basis o f one teacher for up to every group o f 50 pupils. This could
         be achieved through the use o fmulti-grade teaching and mandating a minimum
         class size o f 30 pupils;

    (ii) incentives should be provided to the private sector to play an increasingly
         important role inthe provision of secondary education and increase its share in
         enrollments; and

    (iii)the utilization o f secondary school teacher time should be improvedby employing
         more teachers who teach several subjects, and by training them to teach more than
         one subject.

60.      In summary, the scenariosindicatethat the Governmentneedsto develop
very carefullyitsstrategyfor improvingaccess to secondary education. Major
reforms will also be needed inefficiency and the management o f teachers, and the role o f
the non-Government sector will need to be emphasized. These policy reforms are hlly
consistent with the Government's Letter o f Sector Policy.
