EDUCATIONSECTORGOVERNANCEANDACCOUNTABILITY ACTIONPLAN


INTRODUCTION
Since the initiationof the Free PrimaryEducationinJanuary, 2003the Ministry
of EducationScience and Technology(MOB") hasdevelopedgovernance and
accountability mechanisms that facilitate greater participationof stakeholders in
ensuringtransparency andaccountability. The Ministryhasalsobuiltthe
necessary internal capacity to monitor programmeexpenditureand curriculum
delivery.

The financing of the program, whichis also supported by developmentpartners,
has been through a funds flow system that disburses grants directly to school
bank accounts. The funds earmarked for the procurement of instructional
materials and other school operations are managed by School Management
Committees and monitored by local field officers, DistrictEducationBoards and
other partners and annually audited by our Schools Audit Unit. Consequently,
chances of leakageshave beenhighly reduced thus muringaccountability. This
iscontrary tothe pastexperiencewhenschoolequipmentwas suppliedcentrally,
a process that led to a lot of wastage due to bureaucratic red tape and
mismanagement.

The involvement of the school management committees and FTA in financial
andprocurementmanagement has, withtime, enhancedaccountabilityand built
confidence at the community level. Once funds are receivedat the school level
and expenditures incurred, these are posted at a strategic position inthe school
for all to see. This enables all partners to scnttinize the accounts and see for
themselves the amount received and how ithasbeenused.

The allocation of FPEfunds is on per capita basis, with data beingupdated each
 term through the District Education Office, and confirmed by Instructions
 MaterialsUnitas well as the PlanningUnitof the Ministry. The involvement of a
 number of units provides a mechanism for checks and balances to ensure the
 accuracy of information.

 Initially, a few school heads failed to adhere to the guidelines and were
 disciplined th~oughthe existing disciplinary machinery, Consequently, school
 managements, have been trained andare now able to record and account for the
 funds. Any case5of useof funds contrary to set guidelines are punishedwithout
 delays. The schools that have received overpayments have all refunded the
 money to the MOEST.

 To ensure full accountability, the M O W has its own School Audit Unit whose
 structure starts at the Headquarters down to the districts. The Unit's role is to
 support school level managements in matters pertaining to financial and


                                                                                 2




                                            64

procurement management. In addition, the Ministry has benefited from the
services of boththe normalinternalaudit and an independentauditor contracted
with the concurrence of the Controller and Auditor General to audit Free
Primary Education accounts. The independent auditor performs the task on
behalf of the Controller and Auditor General.           In addition, to assist schools
manage financial and procurement matters, the Ministry has developed the
followingguidelinesandmanuals:

    (a) Nationalpolicyontextbooks
    (b)Primary schoolinstructionalmaterialsmanagementhandbook
    (c) Pnmary schoolsinstructionalmaterialstrainingmanuals
    (d)Suppliesmanual
    (e) Instructionalmaterialsstockreceipt/ issueregister, andorderforms
    (f)Approved list of primary schools textbooks and other instructional
        materials"OrangeBook"
     (g) A handbookof financial managementInstructionsfor PrimarySchools
     (h) FinancialManagementtrainingmanualfor primaryschools

Headteachers and schoolmanagement committees havebeentrained inprocurement and
financialmanagementandplansareundenvayto enhancethis and to makeit continuous.
Inadditionto the developmentof the above guidelinesand manuds, the Ministryhas
alrtady undertaken some specific actions and drawn future plans to ensure proper
governance and accountabilitywithinthe EducationSector. The planhasbemprepared
inthe contextof the GovernmentofKenya'sAntimption ActionPlan. It is basedon
the lessons learnt and activities carried out during the implementation of Free Primary
EducationPrograminthe initialtwo years.

 ThisActionPlanisorganizedaroundthefollowingthems.

 a). Communityinvolvementthroughdecentralizedfinancing and procurement
 b).Resourceallocation
 c). Consultationandsocialaccountability
 d). Publicexpenditureandmanagement
 e). Re-organizationof MOST

 A two-prongapproachhasbeen employedfocusingon

     (i) The progress already made towards a more transparent and
            accountableenvironment
      (ii) Commitmentsforthefuture,

 To sustain transparency and accountability communities and other school level
 stakeholder are directly involved inthe implementationof school activities. To
 achieve the goals, the Ministry is working closely with local level partners to
 ensureconsensusandconfidencebuildingamonglocallevel actors.




                                                 65

Communityinvolvement

OnSchooIgrants, M O W SFreePrimaryEducationProgrammeexperiencehas
demonstrated the effectiveness of transferring financial resources directly to
schools and decentralizing the procurement of instructional materials to that
level. The Kenya EducationSector Support Programme (KESSP) will build on
this experience by continuingwith the school level procurementof instructional
materialsand will extend this throughthe provisionof grantsfor infrastructure
development to School ManagementCommittee(SMCs) and Boardof Governors
(BOGS). There will also be the provision of grants to Early Childhood
DevelopmentEducation(ECDE)committees.

Under the FPESP, funds from Development Partners wiU flow through the
SpecialAccountto the PMG, and thento the MomsProjectAccount. Fromthe
accounts funds wifl be electronicallytransferred to individual school textbook
bank accounts in all the 18,000 public schook in the country. Procurement of
goods and services is done at school level through the School Instructional
Materials committee (SIMC). The Committee chooses books from an approved
list (orange book) of textbooks and other instructional materials and procure
competitively from local booksellers and service providers. Management
committeesare required to holdmeetingswith the FTA to divulge the amountof
 the funds received and the proposed use. It is also mandatory that this
 information is pasted on the school's bulletin board, so that parents and the
 wider community are fuIIy informed and involved in the decision making,
 management and accountability in their school. Local leaders, e.g. politicians
 and sponsors are also encouraged to demand information on the use of
 resources.

 ResourceAIlocation

 The KESSPisdesignedto embracea resourceallocationsystemthat isa propoor
 strategy. For instancepriority for the first phaseof the programis givento basic
 education, which under FPEistargeted to the poor.

 ConsultationandSocial Accountability

  TlrePlanningPhaseincIuded a major processof consultationswith awide range
  of stakeholders (culminating in an Education Conference, held at the end of   -
  2003); in the development of an Education Sessional Paper (approved by
  Parliament in March, 2005); three joint Missions (in September 2004, January
  2005andMay2005, withabout20externalagencies andallnationalstakeholders
  participating) to facilitate effective partnershipsbetween the Government and
  other partners.KESSPProvincialWorkshops were held throughoutthe country
  to present the programme to field implemenkrs. Finally, we jointly discussed
  sector policy and agreed onkey concern and the way forward, hence the letter
  of sector policy.


                                                                                4




                                              66

ClientSatisfaction Survey:

As part of the World Bank's support to Free Primary Education a client
satisfaction survey is b e i i carried out. Such surveys are important vehicles for
measuring the quality of service delivered, because they provide alternative
perspectives to the assessments by Quality Assurance and Standards Officers,
DEOs, Quality Assurance and Standards Officers (QAS), Teacher Advisory
Centre (TAC) Tutors, Head Teachers, Pupils, parents, and school committees
were interviewedinthesurvey.

Public ExpenditureTrackingSurvey:

An advanced draft of this survey has been developed and circulated amongst
MOE" officersfor comments. The survey establishesthe flow andefficiency of
budgetary allocations for instructionai materials and General Purpose
expenditures such as salaries for non-teaching staff and storage of instructional
materials; tracks down to what extent public resources flow from the funding
agency/ies to the Ministry of Finance then to MaEST, to banks, district offices
and eventually Primary Schools. The report will be soon circulated for
comments among key actors. Once circulated it will inform those concerned on
what isexpectedof them.

Communication Strategy;

 While we are proud of the progress made in reaching all the different
stakeholders during the KE.sssPconsultative process, the Ministry recognizesthe
need for a comprehensive strategy that wSl go into the dialogue with key
partners such as teachers unions. For this reason, we plan to develop a
comprehensive strategy to address critical issues and thus create consmus
betweenkey partners.

 PublicExpenditureand Management (MTEFandMPER)

 In recent years, the Government has sought to restructure expenditure
 allocations across sectors, through the medium-tenn expenditure framework
 (MTEF), a three-year programaligned to the Government's Economic Recovery
 Strategy (ERS).The aim of MTEFisto makeexpendituremoreeffective andmore
 alignedto expenditurecategories, withthe highestpotentialto affect growthand
 improve social sector performance. As a result, in 2004, the first Ministerial
 Public Expenditure Review document became available to guide the budget
 processand increaseitstransparency. This was followedup in2005 and officers
 are increasingly becomingaware of what is expectedof them. Inparticular, they
 are allrequiredto ensurereturnoninvestmentsintheir programmeactivities.




                                                                                   S




                                              67

Reorganizationof MoEST:

In order to improve efficiency and performance, the Ministry was recently
reorganized. This entailed (i)competitive recruitment of top managers; (ii}
redistribution of staff away from the HQsto the lower levelsof government and
(iii)introduction of result based managementthrough capacity buildingand
training. All these are aimed at enhancingimplementation capacities of officers
at variouslevels.

XI. COMMITMENTS FOR THE FUTURE
In part 1, above, we have described the actions undertaken this far in order to
promote a more transparent and accountable environment. Through these
approaches, the MOESTintendsto intensifyitscapacity buiIdingprogrammes as
part of itsfight against corruption.The actions discussed inthe followingsection
are time bound and will build on the lessons learnt throughout the
implementation of FPE. They are organized around the same themes as those
discussedinpart 1andsummarizedintable1.

a).   Community involvement through decentralized               financing and
procurement:

 Under primary schoolinfrastructuredevelopmentcomponent,resourcesfor new
 classroomconstructionand school improvement grantswillbe managed at school
 level. In the former case communities do not handle funds directly, but are,
 responsiblefor priority settingand supervisingimplementation accordingto the
 approved guidelines. Beside responding to the objectives of increasing the
 number of classroomsthrough this component, MOEST honours the demands
 from communities not only inconstruction but in all FPE impIementation. To
 empower them the MOJE" hascarried out initialcapacity buiidingtrainingand
 plansmoretraining infuture.

 b). ResourceAllocation

 FPEand other pro-poor componentssuchas secondaryschoolbursary fund and
 school feeding programme are ring fenced. However, we need to enhance
 targetingof bursary allocationto ensure that only the needyreceivesupport.

 Bursariesfor needy studentsie. Orphansand vulnerable childreningeneral, are
 a long-standing feature of the education system in Kenya. Various piecemeal
 reforms have beentried inthe lastfew years inan attempt to improve targeting
 but muchmore needs to bedone. For this reason, we shall sooncommissiona
 review to examine current disbursement modalitiesandrecommendappropriate
 changes.      The TORS for this review have been developed, discussed and
 approved.




                                                                               6



                                             68

At the end of 2004/05 financialyear, we commissioneda survey to find the level
of clientsatisfaction. The resultsfrom this survey will form the basisfor changes
in our service delivery. In addition, there is a proposal to use community
scorecards, in order to get feedback Erom communities on our programme
performance.

Teacher Management

The recent teacher redeployment exercise based on the old staffing norms, will
soonbevalidated and staffing normsrevisedtoensuremoreequitableutilization
of teachers, based onenrolments

Currently MOEST and TSC have parallel databases, with mismatched school
codes and enrolments. This makes validation of data difficult, creates a
conducive environment for corruption (Transparency International recently
ranked Tsc second in a list of agencies with incidences of bribery), and easily
results indata gapsand duplication. To improve this area, we planto mergethe
two databases and to unify the school codes in the process. We also intend to
simplify the school registrationto facilitate and accelerate the mainstreamingof
Non Formal Schools, thus giving the poorer section of the pupils' equal
opportunityof accessto publicresources.

We are aware that initially capitationgrantswere a source of moral hazard, and
 there were evidence of misreportingin some areas. While the MOFSTand TSC
 have tackledthis and reducedchancesof misreportingand refundsmade to the
 Ministry,there is need to continuewith current efforts to eliminate chancesfor
 misreporting. It is therefore, our planto continue devisingthe right structures to
 eliminatethe occurrenceof this phenomenon.

 MOEST and TSC are firmly committed to communicate, coordinate
 cooperate more in information gathering and sharing.          Already we
 witnessed goodprogresstowardsthisdirection.

 Procurement

 We are pleased to have agreed with the development partners that all
 procurement below the threshold for InternationalCompetitive Bidding will be
 carried out in accordance with Government of Kenya Procurement Regulations
 supplementedby a detailed procurementmanual and other relevantdocuments.
 Most of the procurement will be undertaken at the MOEST level (own
 procurement,centrally procureditems e.g., IC"and generaloversight over sub-
 sectoral procurement activities) and at the school/community level. We are in
 agreement with the findings of an EducationSector Procurement Assessment
  carried out by the development partnerswhich found that while the regulations
  provide for a transparent procurementsystem, problemsare emerging with the
  implementation of those rules. At the sehool level, we have began to address

                                                                                  7




                                            69

theseproblems by puttinginplaceanaccountabilityframework. The framework
consists of (i) greater clarity on the rules provided through a detailed
procurement manual, (ii)sensitizingthose involved inprocurement activities on
their roles and accountabilities, (iii)involvingthe communities inoversight over
procurement and (iv) annual procurement audits to identify issues arising
during implementation.

The assessment study also found that the main problems at the Ministry level
relate to the lack of capacity of those implementing procurement activities,
inefficiencies occurring due to, e+ failure of evaluation committees to meet in
time coupled with the requirement fur an overwhelming number of clearances
and mvailability of generalinformationonprocurementactivities.

Inorder to mitigate the identified risksand to ensurethat procurementiscarried
 out efficiently and inaccordancewiththe applicablerules, we will undertakethe
 followingactions:

         Preparation of an annual procurement plan by MOEST which will be
         made public
         Public announcement of all contracts awarded of a value greater than
         above a certain threshold (to beagreed at negotiation) includingwinning
         bidders and contractprice
         Publication of information on contracting activities at the school
         communityIevelinaccordancewith theprocurement manud
         Propermaintenance of allrelevant procurementdocumentation
         Annual external auditsof procurementactivities
         Greater clarity on roles and accountabilities of those involved in the
         procurement function ineludingevaluation committees
         Enhancement of the capacity of those implmenting procurement
         activities
         Enhancement of the capacity of communities to monitor p r o c u m a t
         activities at the schoollevel
         Take actionsto enforceaccountabirityfor actionstaken

 These actions are basically aimed at (i)ensuring adequacy of information of
  procurement activities andtheir outcomes, (ii)clarifying the applicable rulesand
  (iii)building a culture of accountability. It is hopedthat this three pronged
  approach will ensure that procurement is carried out transparently and in an
  efficient manner.

  FinancialManagement:

  Avoidance of bureaucratic processesinthe flow of funds by direct remittance to
  spending units has reduced delays and leakages of resources. Publication of



                                                                                 8



                                                70

remittances to c ~ m u n i t ybased spending units and greater sharing of the
information to facilitatepublic scrutiny and monitoringwill. be enhanced.

Simplification of accountingprocessesby adoption of manuals developed under
FPESP and capacity enhancement at respective spending units will be
strengthened.

Accelerated implementation of riskbasedinternal audit support function, using
government systems will be strengthened. And where necessary the use of
private sector professionals` technical support to promote an effective internal
audit approach (teamingup) to conduct risk basedbiannualaudit reviews will
becontinued.

Supporting effectiveness of the internal function by developing an effective
 ministerial audit committee. This will comprise defining the composition,
 mandate and operational procedures.

 Ensuringtimely conduct of annual financial statementsaudits through capacity
 enhancement of the boththe School Audit Unit (SAW and the Controller and
 Auditor General (CAG)as well as timely follow-up of audit findings through the
 ministerial audit committee andDEB'Swill befollowed.

 Institutionalizing quarterly financid monitoring reporting to all program
 operations, inchding government funds and resources. Quarterly FMRs wiIl be
 subject to joint reviews by all interested parties and significant matters will be
 subjectto ministerial auditcommittee review andaction.

 Jointreview missionswill beguided by a formal risk basedfinancial assessment.

 Finally, good governance in sector operations will be promoted and sustained
 through strongpartnershipsamongallstakeholders.

                          Annex 3: Letter of Sector Policy
                 KENYA: Education Sector Support Project



                                                                                 I




                                 REPUBLICOFKENYA
                           MINISTRY OFFINANCE
Telegraphic Address:22921
FINANCE*NAIR03I                                                          THETREASURY
Telephone: 252299                                                          P.O.Box300W
When replyingpleasequote                                                       NAIROBI
                                                                                KItNYA

Ref. No: EAlFA 63/64/031'8'/(66)                                       August 2,2005

Dr. PaulWolfowitz
President
The World Bank
1818 H Street
WashingtonDC. 20433
USA

Dear       hM#d$J,
                    KENYA EDUCATlON SECTOR SUPPORT PROGRAM
                              LETTEROF SECTOR POLICY

1       As you are aware, Kenya's economic performance during the 1980sand 1990s
        decades fell to a levelfar below its potential. The result of this protractedpoor
        economic performance was increased poverty and serious deteriorationin the
        standardof livingfor a majorityof Kenyans, as reflectedin the worsening of the
        key social indicators, As an example, illiteracy rates increased as enrolment
        rates inprimary school declined.

2       It is against this background that the National Rainbow Coalition (NARC)
        Government on assuming powers in January 2003 introduced Free Primary
        Education (FPE) as part of its endeavours to achieve economic recovesand
        Education for All (EFA) and Millennium Development Goals (MDGs). The
        Governmenttook this bold action in the belief that a well-educatedand health
        population is key to enhancing productivity and overall performance of the
        economy.

3       The Government has made commendable progress in the implementationof
        Free Primary Education programme since its introduction, In the process of



                                         92

   the implementationof this key initiative,we have appreciatedcollaborationand
   supportfrom developmentpartners,in making FPEthe success it has become.
   In particular, the Governmentappreciatesthe contributionsof the World Bank,
   the Department for InternationalDevelopment (DFID), Canadian International
   DevelopmentAgency (CIDA), and Swedish International DevelopmentAgency
   (SIDA), among others, for providing grants to finance the implementation of
   FPE and therefore helping the Governmentto attain its policy goal of realizing
   universal primary education, This new funding system has provided important
   lessonsfor futurefunding of schoolsusing public funds.

4  The financing of FPE has been implemented through a new system of funds
   flow that entails sending grants directlyto school bank accounts for operations
   and procurement of instructional materials. The new approach has relieved
   parents of the various levies, previously paid to finance primary education, As
   a result, many more children from poor families enrolled in primary schools,
   raising enrolment from 5.9 million children in 2002 to 7.2 million in 2003 (an
   increase of 1.3 million children) and from 7.2 million children in 2003 to 7.5
   million in 2005. This rapid increaseraisedthe Net EnrolmentRate (NER) from
  78% in 2003 to 84% in 2004, and pupil to teacher ratio from 34:l to 40:1,
   respectively.

5  The FPE operations at school level are managed by School Management
   Committees (SMCs) comprisingof teachersand parents, Fromour experience,
  the school level implementation of capitation grants is building capacity and
   confidence of SMCs to undertake financial management, procurement of
   goods and services, record keeping and accounting of resources, In addition,
   itlocalbook
       has strengthened local book trade and created a healthy competition among
               traders,

6 Availabilityof textbooksand other instructionalmaterialsIn primary schoolshas
  increased substantially as a result of devolving management of grants and
  procurement to school level. This has improved the textbook-to-pupif ratio in
  most of the schools to about 3:l for standards 1-4 and to 2:l for standards 5-8
  and thus improvedquality of teaching and learning,

7 The Ministry of Education Science and Technology (MOEST)has continuedto
  work closely with development partners, and other education and training


                                                                                 2




                                            93

     stakeholders, through regular consultative meetings on various aspects of the
     provision of education.     In particular, the MOEST has consulted widely,
     includingholdinga stakeholders nationalconferenceon education andtraining,
     which culminated in the preparationof the Sessional Paper (SP) No, 1of 2005
     on Policy Framework for Education, Training and Research, as well as in the
     preparation of the Kenya Education Sector Support Program(KESSP) through
     a Sector Wide Approach (SWAP). The Sessional Paper, which has already
     received Parliamentaryapproval, providesthe blue print for policy direction on
     the provision of quality and relevant educationand training inthe 21" century,
     while KESSP provides a strategic framework for improved access, equity,
     quality and relevance at all levels of educationand training.

Main Sector issues and Government Strategies

8    The Government,working in collaborationwith developmentpartnersand other
     stakeholders, has prepared KESSP investmentwith a view to consolidatingthe
     gains accruingfrom the implementationof FPE. Throughthis initiative, we aim
     to address the main sector issues, strengthening managementand delivery of
     educational services and to improve access, quality, equity and relevance of
     educationand training. In designingthis programme, the Governmentis aware
     that to stay on track towards meeting Economic Recovery for Wealth and
     Employment Creation (ERS), Education for All         (EFA) and MDGs, further
     investments in the short to medium term are necessary. In particular, it is
     critical to work towards ensuring quality and improved access especially for
     about one million school-aged children in the urban slums and Arid and Semi-
     Arid Lands (ASAL), who are still missing.

9    Many childrenin ASAL, Pockets of Povertyand Urban Slums are out of school
     due to lack of schools within walking distance and boarding schools. In
     addition, many primary school buildings are, generally, in a state of poor repair
     andlor lack of enough classrooms and latrines for the increasing numbers of
     children. In many remote areas, school environment is hostileto the growing
     numbers of girls in that the necessary sanitation facilities are not in place. In
     addition, there is need to address the needs of orphans for training and
     learning beyond primary education. There is also need to refocus on special
     Education to afford children with special needs, opportunities to access
     educationand training,


                                                                                     3




                                             94

10 The GoverFment will continue to provide grants to primary schools, including
   the non-formalschools in urban slums, which serve as the re-entry points for
   over-age children and support to other delivery systems such as mobile
   schools in ASALs. The objective is to further improve the supply of textbooks
   in order to sustain the ERS goal of attaining pupiVbook ratio in the lower
   primary to 3:1 and 2:l in the upper primary, respectively, even when numbers
   rise.    The Government wiil also mobilize resources for infrastructure
   development in order to improve existing facilities and to construct new
   classrooms and new schools, especially in poor communities such as in the
   ASAL, Pockets of Poverty and in urban slums. Infrastructuredevelopment will
   be based on schooldevelopment plans, under an infrastructurestrategic plan,
   manuals and guidelines designed to help schools to address their specific
   needs and to contribute effectivelytowards improvinglearning outcomes.

11 The Government will develop a policy based on recent environmental and
   social assessment, to ensure that school infrastructure development takes into
   account the localconditionsof the targetcommunities. The flow, management
   and accountingof funds for infrastructurewill follow the current arrangements
   in use for financing FPE.    In addition to providing for instructional materials
   and infrastructure,the Government will continue to provide in-service training
   for teachers, review the various testing and assessment systems in place in
   order to harmonise them and to establish a national assessment system to
   monitor learning achievements. As a means of improving quality of teaching,
   learning and performance, the Government will also review the existing pre-
   service training programme with a view to rationalizing the current
   programmers of Primary Teacher Colleges as well as the upgrading primary
   school teachers, colleges to diploma level, in the long run. Moreover, in view
   of the need to utilizethe available human resourceseffectively, a study will be
   undertakento inform and guide the restructuringof the training programme to
   ensure that graduates are well prepared and equipped to handle emerging
   challenges, and alternative modes of delivery such as multi-shift and multi-
   grade.

12 There is an urgent need to provide young people with life skills education in
   order to equip them with survival knowledge and skills within a healthy clean
   and secure school environment. The Government will do this by strengthening


                                                                                   4




                                          95

    the teaching of HlVlAlDs education, health and sanitation in all schools and
    ensuring that these are mainstreamed in school programmes. The materials
    for drug abuse, HIVIAIDS, as well as school health and sanitation will be
    included in the school instructionalmaterials' list, in the Orange Book, as part
    of the approved instructionalmaterials,with appropriate instructionsto schools
    to purchase them. The implementationof the HIVIAIDS education will benefit
    from the lessons learnt from the on-going pilots and enhanced guidance and
    counseling programmes in schools, Primary school teachers will continue to
    receive regular and in-servicetraining for professionalgrowthto enable them
    to effectively deliver the new curriculum, undertake school based assessment
    of learning achievements, as well as in the use of alternative approaches to
    curriculumdelivery,

13. The main issues and challenges to be addressed in the Early Childhood
    Development Education (ECDE) include the need for a comprehensive policy,
    enhanced access, improved financing and training of teachers.               The
    Governmentwill continueto addressthese issues within its policy of promoting
    the development and management of ECDE through partnership with
    households, communities and the privatesector. On its part, the Government
    will work with stakeholders to develop an overall ECDE policy that will
    incorporatea legal and comprehensive national ECDEframework to guide the
    development and management of all ECDE programmes, In addition, the
    Government will intensify programmes for capacity building and resource
    mobilizationto empowercommunitiesand other stakeholdersto develop ECDE
    facilities and to provide effrcient management. In particular, the Government
    will provide, within KESSP investment, support for training of teachers and
    targeted grants to poor communities to expand and improve ECDE services,
    based on agreed criteria, costs and implementation plans, in order to ensure
    benefitsto all the ECDE learners.

14, For the secondary sub-sector, there are several major concerns. In 2003,
    transition from primary to secondary school was at 47%, gross enrolment rate
    stood at 29% and the retention rate at about 79%. In addition, increased
    numbersof pupilsgraduating from the primaryschool level is putting increased
    pressure on the limited secondary school places.           The Gross Enrolment
    Ratelpupit-Teach Ratio (GEWPTR) will be raised and class sizes increased to
    40  -45    students per class. Secondary schools, especially boarding ones,


                                                                                   c




                                            96

   remain too expensive to parents, who traditionally have been and continue to
   be responsiblefor the development of secondary schools, In spite of providing
   for school development, parents do not play a major role in school
   management. The consequence of this situation has been inadequate
   community ownership of schools in their areas, especially those that admit
   children from provincial and national levels. Current management structures
   will need to be reviewedto allow greater parental participation. There is also
   need to strengthen the management capacity of the Board of Governors
   (BOGS) and legally incorporate Parents-TeachersAssociation (PTAs) in line
   with the new policy. increased poverty levels in the country have hampered
   parents' capacity to expand and develop secondaryschools. Other constraints
   include inadequate science facilities and equjpment, hence, poor performance
   in sciences and mathematics subjects especially by gids. For reasons to do
   with past experiencesthere is inefficientutilizationof teachers, with a low PTR
   of 191, gender and regional disparities exist that fimit the education of girls,
   and a bursary programme whose review is necessary to improve its
   management to ensure transparent targeting in support to orphans and
   studentsfrom poor families.

15 The Government will promote a more efficient development of the secondary
   sub-sector in order to improve access, equity and quality of secondary
   education.    The Government will also promote full utilization of any idle
   capacity in secondary schools by raising class enrolments to between 40-45
   students; adding more streams, as appropriate, to existing small schools, that
   is, those with less than three streams, and promoting the establishment of day
   schoolsto reducecost to parents, The Governmentwill also undertakea study
   of the current secondary school bursary system in order to ensure
   transparency in allocation, management, coordination, targeting criteria and
   equity. Special efforts will be made to target orphans and giris from poor
   households. In addition, the Governmentwill provide targeted support to poor
   areas with low enrolments, especially in the ASALs and urban slums, through
   collaboration with communities, BOGS and other stakeholders, where
   necessary new schools will be built in such areas. Other measures to be
   implemented will include activities aimed at enhancing the quality of teaching,
   learning and performance, especially in sciences and mathematics at Kenya
   Certificateof Secondary Education(KCSE) level.



                                                                                  6




                                         97

    A study of secondary education sub-sector will be undertaken to provide the
    basis for further reformsinsecondary sub sector. Oncethe study is completed,
    strategieswill be designedto addressthe challenges and guide investments.

17. In the case of special education, the Governmentwill enhance the provisionof
    education to persons with various challenges to help them become self-reliant
    and contribute to national development. Some of the sub-sector issues to be
    addressedinclude the need for a clear national strategy and updated data on
    disabilities to support appropriate specialized training, provision of equipment
    as well as instructional materials for the special education programmes in
    mainstreamed cases and special schools. The Government wilt, therefore,
    undertake a national survey on disabilities and develop a national strategic
    plan to comprehensiveiy identify and define specific disabilities and related
    needs and collaboratewith other providers of special education to meet such
    needs.

18  tn addition, the Government will develop and standardize diagnostic
    assessment tools to facilitate identification and placement of learners with
    special needs. Identification of special needs will help in the integration of
    special education programmes in all teaming and training institutions and
    ensure that such institutionsare responsiveto specific needs of learners, The
    Government will also waive duty on specialized equipment and materials and
    provide incentivesfor local production of such equipment as a way of reducing
    the cost of providing special education. The Government will further ensure
    that Kenya Institute of Special Education (KISE) has the capacity to offer pre
    and in-servicetraining COUFSBSfor teachersof special needs education.

    The Government recognizesthe important role played by Adult and Continuing
    Education (ACE) as well as Non-Formal Education (NFE). These avenues
     provide entry and re-entry opportunitiesfor children outside the formal school
    systemto benefitfrom education andtraining and thus helpingthe Government
    to achieve EFA and MDGs. To enhance the role of these institutions, the
    Government will develop a national qualification framework that wilt provide
    opportunities for linkage between NFE and the formal education and training
    systems, thus creating re-entry at all levels.        The Government will also
    collaborate with varr'ous providers and stakeholders to mobilize resources to
    support ACE and Non-Formal Schools (NFS), register all NFE centers, build


                                                                                   7




                                          98

    capacity for management, develop and regularly review their curriculum to
    ensure appropriatenessand linkage with the formal system. The Government
    will also conduct regular literacy surveys and register eligible learners for
    nationalexaminationsand providetraining opportunitiesto instructors.

20  The higher education sub-sector, including Technical IndustrialVocational and
    Entrepreneurship Training (TIVET) and universities is in need of
    comprehensivereview and reformstrategiesto enhancethe roles of the public
    and private sectors in the developmentof education and training at this level.
    The TIVET sub-sector,in particular, lackseffective nationalco-ordinationof the
    many programs offered by various institutions and providers. The two sub-
    sectors also suffer from inadequate and obsolete equipment, a mismatch of
    skills taught with market demands, limited access due to inadequatefacilities,
    highcost of educationand training, inadequatefunding for researchand lack of
    a unified accreditation system and harmonized legal frameworks.            With the
    approval of the new policy framework, the Government will now address the
    existingweaknesses inthe existing legal framework.

21. The Government will encourage greater partnershipswith the private sector in
    the development and expansion of TIVET and university education. To this
    end, the Governmentwill work with partnersin devising mechanismsarid apply
    appropriate incentives to promote private sector investments in the
    development of TIVET and university education. It will also establish an
    autonomous coordinatingauthority for TIVET programmescurrently offered by
    various institutions and providers, The establishment of the authority and
    national skills training strategy will be done in consultation and full involvement
    of key stakeholders,
                              I




22. Scholarships and other merit awards for staff and students will be established
    to promote excellence in creativity and innovation in the field of science and
    technology, In the process, loans and bursariesto enhance access to TIVET,
    paying attentionto marginalizedgroups such as female students and students
    with disability.    To enhance and improve transition from tertiary (TIVET) to
    university level, appropriate linkages and credit transfers will be established
    between TIVET institutions and universities.            Towards this goal, the
    Government will first develop a national skills training strategy, in the first
    phase of implementing KESSP investment plans for TIVET, with a view to


                                                                                      8




                                               99

    undertakeessential reforms and the promotion of private sector participationin
    the provisionof TNET.

23, For the university education, the Government will provide a framework for
    sustainable, competitive and autonomous university system, whose main
    expansion will be mainly through greater private sector investment, and
    promoted in tandem with population growth and the demand fur university
    education. The Government will provide incentives to the private sector to
    invest more in university education and provide scholarshipsto students based
    on the needs of the economy, and targeted bursariesand loans to the needy,
    taking into account gender and regionalparity.

24. The Governmentwill also review and harmonizeexisting legislation to establish
    effectivecoordination of universityeducation. To this end, the Commissionfor
    Higher Education (CHE) will be strengthened to carry out its functions
    effectively and to assure quality in all higher education institutions,
    Mechanisms will also be developed for universities to undergo regular peer
    reviews and evaluation of their programmes in order to ensure quality and
    relevance in education and training, thus address the current skills mismatch.
     In implementingthese strategies, the Government will frrst develop a national
    strategic framework definingthe respective roles of the state and private sector
    in the provision of university education; and reconcile university investment
     programmeswith availableresources.

25,  Efficient utilization of available resources is critical to the attainment of sector
     goals.    In order to achieve this, the Government will ensure efficient
     management and utilization of teachers, who form an important resource in
     teaching and learning. This is particularly critical for pilmary and secondary
     school teachers who are the bulk of the teaching force, and whose wage bill
     accounts for over 80% of the MOEST annual budgetary allocation. A key
     strategy will be to deploy teachers equitably and utilize them more efficiently
     and, at the same time ensure that the wage bill does not continue to escalate
     rapidly.




                                                                                       9




                                            100

26, In order to utilize primary teachers more equitably the Teachers Service
    Commission (TSC) has undertakena teacher balancing exercise by deploying
    teachers from over-staffed regionsldistrictslschoolsto those that have been
    under-staffed. The Governmentrecentlycommissioneda stafflng norms study
    whose recommendationswill be used to establish new staffing norms that will
    improve teacher utilization.The TSC will adopt new staffing norms for primary
    schools based on enrolments. On the basis of this, the TSC will deploy a
    teacher to teach 45-50 pupils in urban and high potential areas, while for
    smaller classes, with lower enrolments, especially in areas with sparse pupil
    catchments, teachers will be trained to use alternativedelivery modes such as
    multi-gradeteaching, The Government will also examine ways and means of
    linkingteacher promotionsto performance.

27, At secondary level, TSC will establish new staffing norms and use these to
    deploy the current stock of teachers lo schools based on Curriculum-Based
    establishment(CBE). The Government will increasethe current teaching load
    to a minimum teaching load of between 25 to 30 hours per week, including20
    hoursof class instructionand 5 hoursof subject basedco-curricularactivities.

28  The Government is also concerned about the low pupil teacher ratio for
    secondary education, which stands at 19.1. For this reason there will be no
    hiring of new teachers except to replace those who leave service due to
    attrition or to address specific needs.    However, to ensure adequacy of
    teachersthis situation will be reviewedon an annual basis. In the meantime,
    the Ministry is carrying out a field survey to establish actual teacher needs,
    paying specialattentionto teachers inthe payrollthat may be either too sick or
    on study leave. Once this information is out we shall share it with our partners
    and deliberateon the next courseof action on the issueof teacher recruitment.
    For teachers whose subjects are no longer on the timetable, several options
    exist for their appropriate deployments, for example, retraining, and
    deployment in primary schools.      The BOGSwill also be allowed to employ
    contract or part time teachers to meet short-term teacher shortages and
    specific local needs, The Government will also review the policy for offering
    optional subjects and to require a minimum number of students before a
    course is offered. The teachers of optional subjects will also be deployed to
    selected schools to enhance meeting the standard staffing norms, and where



                                                                                 10




                                          101

    they cannot reach the agreed teaching loads, they will be employed on part
    time basis or be requiredto teach in morethan one school,

29, In order to ensure smooth implementation of sector reforms and in particular
    staffing norms, the Government will enter in dialogue with the Kenya National
    Union of Teachers (KNUT) and other relevant labour unions. The purpose of
    consultations will be to sensitize the teaching force and key stakeholders on
    the rationalefor the implementation of new staffing norms in order for them to
    accept and support reforms in teacher management, This will not only stabilize
    the wage bill but also maximize resource utilization.

30. Allocationof education resources will also be done more systematically,within
    the requirements of Medium Term Expenditure Framework (MTEF), and
    stringent measures taken to control education expenditures through efficient
    management and utilization of available resources. As part of expenditure
    control, the Government will not increase the number of teachers untii those in
    employment are fully utilized. To sustainbasic education, the Governmentwill
    ensure adequate funding for this sub-sector and will allocate at least 55% of
    the recurrenteducation budget to primary education, with 15% being allocated
    to non-salaryschool level operations.

31  To enhance resource utilization,and strengthen accountability, the Ministrywill
    continue to empower school level management bodies with decision-making
    authoritythrough a harmonized legislativeframework. Itwill further continue to
    build the capacity of local level stakeholders to empowerthem make demands
    on school heads and also watch over those who manage schools and other
    resources.     In particular, stakeholders from among the communities will
    continueto receivetraining on good practices in the implementationof the Free
     Primary Education Programmeand the KESSP. This will enhance community
    ownership and hence minimize possible corruption in the implementation of
    educationactivitieslprogrammes.

32, The Ministry will strengthen advocacy programmes and communication
    between headquarters and the implementing units and all stakeholders. Field
    Officers will be responsible for coordinating education activities and
    communicationwith stakeholders.       The use of circulars, guidelines, manuals,
    the media and face-to-face meetings will be intensified for effective




                                          102

    communication and to help in building the necessary consensus and
    confidenceamong the various actors. In addition, the Governmentwill develop
    a communication strategy that will strengthen cooperation and collaboration
    with stakeholders and partners and endeavour to adequately provide
    information based on Education Management Information System (EMlS).
    Ultimately, increased sharing of information will enhance transparency and
    accountability inthe delivery of educationservices.

33, The Governmentwill improvethe capacity and effectivenessof MOESTand its
    institutions, especially at the district and school levels where most education
    activities and resources wiIl be concentrated. The objective of capacity
    building will be to ensure effective training, implementation and monitoring of
    education activities. An EMlS that is district based and networked between
    MOEST and its agencies will be established to facilitate flow of information
    betweenthe schools, district and MOEST headquartersas well as its agencies,
    especiallythe TSC, Kenya Instituteof Education(KIE) and the Kenya National
    Examination Council (KNEC),         This will enhance timeliness of data flow
    betweenkey and agencies and thus improvemonitoringof field implementation
    and expenditurecontrol.

34  The MOEST has undertaken an internal reorganizationand established a new
    position of Education Secretary and five new divisions headed by Directors,
    that is, Basic Education; Higher Education, TIVET; Policy and Planning; and
    Quality, Assurance and Standards, which are aimed at strengthening the
    management of education and training. For the first time inthe Minktry, these
    key positions were filled through a transparent and competitive recruitment
    process, A further comprehensive review and restructuring is under way for
    the entire ministerial structure, paying attention to staffing needs and priorities
    based on its expanded responsibilities, tn the process of this review, the
    Ministry aims to put in place professional and accouniable management
    structures that will deliver better quality education services to all Kenyans,
    especially the poor in rural and other marginal areas. The management of
    decentralized services will be strengthened by establishing a comprehensive
    and harmonized legislativeand managementframework that will give decision-
    making authority and accountability to field level officers, especially at district
    and school level managementbodies.



                                                                                     12




                                               103

35,  The MOEST will work closely with all public and private sector education
     stakeholders without discrimination or marginalization. In particular, the
     Ministry aims at building adequate capacities for school and field level quality
     assurance. Inthis regard, schools will be expected to ensure quality curriculum
     deliveryat alltimes.

36.  The KESSP investment will strengthen the management reforms being
     undertakenby the Ministry and thus help improve the quality of the delivery of
     services at all levels. The implementationof KESSP will be rnainstreamed
     within the various divisions of the various Directorates in the MOEST
     headquarters and carried out in field offices, especially at district and school
     levels.   Its processes will also be fully integrated into the MOEST's
     decentralized systems and accompanied by appropriate strategic plans,
     guidelines,manuals and targetedcapacity buildinginitiatives.

37.  Mr. President, in conclusion, let me reiterate the Kenya Government's
     appreciation of the financial assistance the Bank has continued to give to
     supp rt our development programmes. We are committedto continue working
     closl&$with the Bank and the other Development Partners in order to realize
     our na ronal vision of making "Kenya a Better Home for AH". We assure you
     the support we are seeking to finance this particular education programme
     would be used prudently, We are confident successful implementation of this
     programmewill contributegreatlyto the realizationof our nationalvision.


Yours




HON. DAVID MWIRARIA, EGH, MP
MINISTER FOR FINANCE

                          Annex 6: DetailedProjectDescription
                       KENYA: EducationSector SupportProject


KESSP's 23 IPSare listedbelow, groupedby area o f improvement. For each, there is a
brief description o f activities. There is also an estimated cost expected to be neededto
achieve KESSP objectives. However, the costs would be reviewed annually.

I. Ensuringequityofaccesstobasiceducation.Theinvestmentprogramsto
achieve this objective are aimed at reducing disparities ineducation opportunity due to
wealth, geographical location and gender, by creating an opportunity for all children
(including those o f the poorest families andthose living inremote areas) to attend a
school o f acceptable quality.

1     Primary school infrastructure (US$SS.3 million).

      Provision o f grants for the purpose of: (a) rehabilitating dilapidated schools and
constructiono f additional facilities inexisting schools inpoorest areas; and (b)
constructingo f new primary schools inareas where there i s overcrowding inexisting
schools andor where there are no primaryschools.

      The objective o fthis program is to improve access by constructing o fnew primary
schools inareas where there is overcrowding inexisting schools andwhere there are no
primaryschools. The distribution o fnewprimaryschools will bebased on priority needs
across the country and will be identifiedprimarilyon a comprehensive analysis o f the
2004 school mapping data but other interim interventioncriteria may apply. Based on
the school mappingdata, the districts andmunicipalitiesthat are overcrowded andhave
the highest demand for new schools will be selected and ranked. At the same time,
schools with the longest distance apart will be identifiedand ranked.

      The overall management o fthe constructionprogram will be determined inthe data
analysis andplanningstage. Detailed guidelines will be developed to operationalize this
program, many o f which will be the same as those developed for the school improvement
grants. Procedures will follow Government's and development partners' procedures for
tendering, construction, contract management and quality control and will support the
MOE's ongoing reforms to decentralize decision-making. Alternative methods o f service
deliverywill be developedduringthe design process. While districts andmunicipalities
will be selected centrally; need andlocationfor schools will bedone at the district level.
Functioning SMCs, PTAs and approved development plan will be necessary prior to
construction o fnew schools.

2     Non-formal schools (US$25.9 million)

       Strengthening the capacity o fnon-formal schools through: (a) developing a
curriculum on non-formal education; (b) providing grants for instructionalmaterials to
NFSs andNFECs and rehabilitation or constructiono f infrastructure; (c) introducing non-


                                               113

formal education management information systems; (d) buildingmanagement capacity;
and (e) mainstreaming and coordinating non-formal schools and centers.

3     Special needs education (US$20 million)

(c) teacher training; (d) providing o f grants for special needs education; (e) promoting
advocacy and awareness creation; and (0 financing o f operating costs.

5     Expanding education opportunities inarid and semi-arid lands (US$1.5 million)

      Expanding educational opportunities in arid and semi-arid lands through the
establishment o f mobile schools.

11.     Enhancingquality andlearningachievement. The investmentprograms to
achieve this objective are to ensure that a set o f essential inputs i s available to every
child, that these inputs are effectively utilized, that learning i s the central focus o fwhat i s
happeninginthe classroom, that learningprogress is regularly measured, and that the
quality o f schooling i s effectively monitored and supported. Also included inthis
category are teacher development and communication technology ineducation.

8      Primary school instructionalmaterials (US$474.9 million)




                                               114

       The basic decentralized instructional materials funding system on which the current
FPESP instructionalmaterials procurement system i s based, was pioneeredby a pilot
project supported by the Dutch Government, which was launched inLaikipia and
Machakos Districts in 1996. From 1999 untilthe present, the pilot project initiatives
(which also resulted inthe current national primary textbook policypublishedin 1998)
were further developed by the GovernmentDFID SPRED 3 project, which extended the
same basic principles to every district inKenya. The current FPESP i s therefore the
logical development o f a continuous andwell tested set o freforms intextbook financing,
provision, procurement, usage and management, which has beenunderway for the past
seven years since 1996. There are now successful, established programs for the provision
o f textbooks andother materials at primary level through the SIMBA account. The
national student:textbook ratios inschools are now ranging 1:2 to 1:4 (the target o f 1:3)
inlower primary schools and 1:2 to 1:4 (the target of 1:2) inupperprimary schools.
However the ratios vary betweendistricts. Insome schools the targets have already been
achieved while ina few they havebeen exceeded. With regardto the impact o f the
textbooks andteacher guides on teaching and learning, there is evidence from various
studies that there have beenvery positive effects. These include: (i)     student retention and
attendance have improved; (ii)    students like the newbooks and their school performance
has improved; and (iii)    teachers find it easier to teach, can convey skills and knowledge
more quickly andcan give homework assignments.

        The components under primaryschools instructionalmaterials investment program
will include:

        Provision o f instructionalmaterials;
        Provision o f funds for general purposes funds;
        Reprinting o f instructionalmaterials; and
        Provision o f stationery and equipment.

        Objective. The objective i s to provide all public primary schools andNFS with
instructionalmaterials inorder to increase access and address quality.

        Investment Strategy. Each enrolled pupil inpublic primary schools will receive,
through Account I,    K Sh430 peryear for instructionalmaterials. Thejustification for
this isto purchasenew textbooks since not all schools had achieved the ratios o f 1:2 and
 1:3, taking into account appreciation inprices, replace andreplenish lost and worn out
textbooks, provide for standard4 and 8 textbooks to cater for the new curriculum,
provide one textbook for every subject to every child bythe end o f year 2010 andprovide
exercise books, pencils, dusters, chalk, registers, charts and wall maps. The disbursement
o f these funds shall be done twice a year.

        Each enrolled pupilinpublic primary schools will receive, through General
Purpose Account (GPA), K Sh 370 per year for general expenses. These funds will
enable schools meet the following expenditures throughout the year; support staff wages,
repair, maintenance and improvement o f school buildings,pay for pupils activities at
school level, meet quality assurance expenses that include school based examinations,



                                                115

meet travel expenses for school teachers and others involved inthe management, pay for
electricity, water and conservancy, pay for postagebox rental and telephone andmeet
contingency expenses.

       Also, each enrolled pupil inregistered NFS will receive, through Account I, KSh
650.00 per year for two years and K Sh 430.00 per year for the next three years for
instructional materials. Inaddition, each pupil inregistered N F S will receive K Sh
370.00per year for five years for general expenses inschool.

The management o fthis program falls under the Directorate o f Basic Educationwithin
the MOE. However, the unit that i s currently managingthe FPEprogram will do the day-
to-day management o f this program.

9      In-service Primary Teacher Education (US$2 million)

       The M O E has, through its INSET Unit,beenrunningschools-based distance
education INSET programs since 2001. From 2001-04 the INSET unitran a successful
program called the Schools-based Teacher Development program (SbTD). The SbTD
was one component of the MOEST Strengthening Primary o f Education (SPRED 2)
Project 11, supported by DFID. The aims of the SbTD were primarilyto `improve the
quality and cost effectiveness o f teaching and learning inprimaryschools on an equitable
basis'. This was to be achieved through `teachers usingnew skills that promote active
learning andthrough the use o fnew textbooks provided under the project' (SPRED I1
Project Memorandum Log-frame, October 1996: 2). The intention was to improve the
teaching and learning environment inall schools inthe country.

       The SbTD program reached and successfully graduated over 50,000 primary school
teachers throughout Kenya, in-servicing them usinga distance led teacher education
scheme inthree subjects, English,maths and science (MOEST, 2004). As a result, three
teachers from every school, called Key Resource Teachers (KRTs) were in-serviced
through the program. Primaryheadteachers were not eligible for the SbTD program.
The program was supported by the Zonal-based teacher advisory system involving over
1,000 Teacher Advisory Centre Tutors (TACs). The TAC tutors worked out o f Teacher
Advisory Centres (TACs) located mainly inprimary schools ineach o f the 1,052 zones in
the country. The TAC tutors were able to provide an effective group-based support
service to the KRTs who were working with their distance learning materials while
carrying a full-time teaching load inthe schools. The TACs, along with the primary
school head-teachers, were one o f the key reasons for the success o f the program.

       Inresponse to the special challenges ofthe FPEpolicyintroduced inJanuary 2003,
the Government decided to extend the INSETprogram to include the training o f an
additional 36,000 KRTs (18,500 Kiswahili KRTs and 18,500 Guidance and Counselling
teachers - one each in every school inthe country). Further, the MOE i s also carrying
out a second national program of school based development called the School
Empowerment Program (SEP). The SEP program i s primarilyaimed at improving
management and children's learning effectiveness inthe school. The SEP involves head



                                             116

teachers, deputyhead teachers and senior teachers, working together with the graduate
KRTs from the first SbTD cycle to plan, design andrunan annual program o f school-
based INSET designed to help teachers respond effectively to the special challenges o f
the new Free Primary EducationPolicy andto any future emerging developments. Both
the SbTD Extension and SEP are beingimplemented in2005.

       The MOEproposes a 5-year investment strategy to continue to runthree programs
which it has now developed with new cohorts o f teachers. The programs will in-service
additional groups o f teachers ineachprogram; SbTD - 60,000 new KRTs (2006-09);
SEP 10,000 and 18,500 / DHT (2006-09) andCluster-based inservicing o f teachers
(Standard1-4) for 32,000 teachers.

10     Pre-service teacher education (US$18.3 million)

 11    Quality assurance and standards (US$26.5 million)

       Subject-based content mastery andpedagogical skills up grading; quality

activities; harmonizationo ftraining inlearning achievement studies; establishment o f an

for the Kenya National Examination Council (KNEC); examination review for adult

andmaterial for non-science students; ABE for Kenya Certificate ofPrimary Education
(KCPE) andKenya Certificate o f Secondary Education (KCSE) equivalent; curriculum
 support material production and induction o fteachers; student achievement monitoring;
andalternative teachindlearning approaches.

18     HIV/AIDS(US$56.6 million)
The KESSPhas significantly addressedthe internal andexternal aspects o f
mainstreamingHIV/AIDS mitigation ineducation inline with the Government's Policy
on HIV/AIDS. Duringthe next five years the M O E aims to protect the education
infrastructure against the impact o f HIV/AIDS and to maximize its potential to both
prevent further infection andmitigate against the most damaging effects o f the HIV
pandemic. The main strategies are to: (i)   maximize the potential strengths o f education
institutions inall sectors to help prevent new infections; (ii)extend evidence-based
initiatives to the widest scale possible, whilst maintaining effectiveness; (iii)
                                                                                utilize
existing training capacity and teaching and learning resources strategically and cost-
effectively; (iv) protect, care and provide support to the most vulnerable, including
orphans, girls, those out o f school and those with special needs ineducation; (v) lead
others indeveloping strong multi-sectoralpartnerships; (vi) mobilize education service
providers to implement the HIV/AIDS policy to the full and inconjunction with other
sectors; and (vii) strengthen and extend the response to the prevention and care needs of
education service providers.

        The following components will be carried out: (i)  prevention - in-service in
primary schools; in-service insecondary schools and sub sectors; peer supporter
initiatives inTTCs and technical institutions; orientation on KIEmaterials; provision o f
teaching and learning materials insub sectors and provision o f school health club activity
kit; (ii) and support - financial support to orphans; extended families support;
         care
apprenticeship scheme for child heads o f families; bigbrothedsister mentoring; video
shows on abuse, stigma, discrimination, harassment, sexual abuse, drug dependency,
barriers to inclusion, retention and transition; (iii)
                                                    workplace issues - strengthening
establishment o f Teachers Living With HIVIAIDS (TLWHA)networks, video on
implementing the education sector policy on HIV/AIDS inthe workplace andrevision
anddistribution o fguide on regulations; and(iv) management o fresponse - strengthening
AIDS Control Unitsand supporting university ACUs inHIV/AIDS and drug dependency
initiatives.

 19     Capacity building(US$l 1million)

        Capacity buildingand ICT training for headquarter and semi autonomous
Government agencies (SAGAS) staff; capacity buildingfor provincial and district
education office staff; primary school capacity building; ECD, NFS, HIV/AIDS, gender,
school health and feeding program guidance and counseling; TMU capacity building;
relocationo f Kenya Education Staff Institute (KESI); secondary school management and

20      Education management information system (US$6.9 million)

management; (c) establishing LocalArea Networks (LANS) for information sharing; and



                                              119

21     Teacher management (US$0.25 million)

       Activities under this IPinclude: (i)  redefining o f TSC agency functions. The
relevant Acts related to the performance o f core functions o fthe TSC will berevised with
a view to legalizing some o f the agencies that are currently not legally recognized, such
as, DEBSand BOGS;(ii)      capacity building for teachers, districts and TSC headquarters.
Under this sub-component, adequate capacity to perform devolved hnctions will be
developed at the headquarters, districts and schools; (iii)  efficient teacher deployment and
utilization system. Under this sub-component, TSC will implement the recommendations
o f the on-going study on staffing norms; (iv) Monitoring and evaluation. Since teacher
management i s a sensitive issue, TSC will regularly and closely monitor and evaluate the
implementation o fthe intendedreforms with a view to review.

22     Gender and Education InvestmentProgram (US$6.4 million)

(andboyswhere applicable), girls' participation andperformanceineducation, water and

Introduction

1.     This annex is structured inthree sections. Section One discusses KESSP
coordination, SectionTwo KESSP implementation, and SectionThree KESSP
accountability.

include:

    National EducationAdvisory Council.

0   Inter-ministerial coordination through an Inter-Ministerial Committee on Education
    and Training.

0   Provincial andDistrict coordination through Provincial and District Education
    Boards.

education service provision duringKESSP implementation.
