2. KESSP Imidementation

5.      Inorder to successfully operationalize the KESSP,itis essentialthat effective
management structures are put inplace. To address this issue, the M O E will replicate the
successful management model established under the Primary School Textbook Program,
which was supported by the Government and development partnersover a number o f
years. Key to the success of the Primary School Textbook Programwas the
establishment o fthe TMU, whereby a small team o fprofessional and support staffwere
givensole responsibility for implementing all aspects ofthis program. As important, was
the provision o f sufficient office space and equipment (computers, printers, office
furniture). The T M U i s a very good model to successfully operationalize the KESSP for
two reasons. First, the TMU fits well within the new management reforms which have
been undertakenat the MOE. Second, the T M U has demonstrated that a dedicated unit
can manage and account for large amounts o f Government/development partner
investments.

6.      Replicating the best-practice lessons from the TMUhas started inthe MOE with
the establishment o fmanagement units for each o fthe investment programs listedunder
the KESSP (as listed above). This does not require a major restructuring at the head
office, but rather it has involved the renaming o f existing offices and the reassignment o f
staff within the MOE. To streamline reporting, supervision, and coordination, the Team
Leader o f each Management Unit will report directly to their Director. To support the
successful establishment o fthe above management units a special capacity building
training program has beenproposed under the Capacity BuildingInvestmentProgram
listed inthe KESSP.

7.      Support to the Directors and Management Unitsinthe MOEwill be provided by
existing Units and offices underthe area of Central Support Services, these are the:


                                              133

Central PlanningUnit;HumanResource Management Unit; School Audit Unit;Finance;
Administration; Accounts; andProcurement.

3. KESSPPerformanceAccountability

8.     Inorderto successfully implementtheKESSPitisessentialthat effective
performance accountability mechanisms are put inplace so that MOE and MOST staff
working at various levels can be held to account for the delivery o f quality education
services to Kenyancitizens across the country. Also important, is the need to share
information on a regular basis bothwithin the MOE, andwith external stakeholders, to
ensure that all are fully informed as to the progress on KESSP implementation.

9.      Inthe diagrampresented above, anoverall information sharingandperformance
accountability structure i s proposed for the implementation o f the KESSP. This structure,
which combines both vertical and horizontal accountability mechanisms, builds on
international best practice inthe delivery of essential basic services. Furthermore, the
proposed mechanisms support the implementationof a number o f policies outlined inthe
Sessional Paper 2005, such as establishing a national mechanism for consultation and
coordination o f all education service providers. The proposedvertical andhorizontal
accountability mechanisms listed inthe above diagram are described below.

(a) Verticalaccountabilitymechanisms

(i) Fromfrontline serviceproviders to theMinister of Education

10.     This is the traditional accountability mechanism, already inplace, whereby
frontline service providers, such as head teachers o fprimary schools, account to their
District Education Office (DEO) who then accounts upwardsinthe organizational
hierarchy to the Provincial EducationOffice, Division Directors, the Education Secretary,
the Permanent Secretary and lastly, the Minister. As indicated by the arrows shown in
the diagram below, this i s the main accountability mechanism inthe proposed
coordination and performance accountability structure.

 11.    Itis important to highlight that this accountability mechanismhasbeen
strengthened recently by important reforms which have beenundertaken at the MOE.
This has involved changing the name o fthe Inspectorate Division andredefining its
mandate so that it i s now responsible for Quality, Assurance and Standards among all
service providers. This Divisionwill play an important role inproviding information to
education managers as to the performance o f education service providers across the
education sector.

(ii) From District Education Boards to Provincial Education Boards to theKESSP
Steering Committee

 12.    Inorder to ensure that the traditionalvertical accountability mechanismfunctions
effectively, it i s essential that an alternative mechanismbe inplace to provide



                                                 134

complementary independentinformation to senior managers at the head office inthe
MOE. Inthe above diagram, the KESSP Reform Secretariat will engage directly with
Provincial and District EducationBoards inorder to provide alternative sources o f
information to senior MOEmanagers who are members o f the KESSP Steering
Committee. This Committee, which will be chaired by the Education Secretary, andwill
consist o f Directors and other senior managers, will meet on a monthly basis to review
KESSP implementation. This mechanismwill play an important role inensuring that
senior managers at the head office have a full picture as to the performance o fDEOs and
frontline service providers ineach district.

(iii) From OversightManagement Committees to DEBs

13.     A thirdvertical accountability mechanisminvolves coordination andreporting
between Oversight Management Committees andDEBs. Inorder to successfully
implement the KESSP, it i s essential that there i s a clear link between Oversight
Management Committees, such as School Management Committees, Parent Teacher
Associations, Boards o f Governors, and DEBs.
This will ensure that DEBs can access independent information as to the performance o f
frontline service providers within each district.

(iv) From KESSP Management Units to Inter-Agency Committees

 14.    A fourth vertical accountability mechanisminvolves reportingbymanagers o f
KESSP management units to their colleagues who are members o f Inter-Agency
Committees which have been established to support the successful implementationo fthe
KESSP. A good example o f such a partnership canbe seen inthe Inter-Agency
Coordination Committee which has already been established bythe MOE and the
MinistryofHealth(MOH) to coordinate schoolhealthinterventions. Inorder to
successfully implement the School Health, Nutrition and Feeding Investment Program in
the KESSP, it is essential that there is close coordinationbetween the MOE, the MOST
 andthe MOH, as well as other Government organizations, such as the Kenya Medical
Research Institute (KEMRI). Active information sharing and accountability between
M O E management units and Inter-Agency Coordination Committees will also be
 important for the successful implementationo fthe HIV/AIDS, Adult Basic Education,
 and TIVET investment programs.

 (b) Horizontalaccountability mechanisms

 15,    Horizontal accountability mechanisms enable "local" accountability relationships
 to operate between service providers and users o f education services, such as parents. In
 the above diagram four horizontal accountability mechanisms are shown. These are:

 (i) Between OversightManagement Committees and Service Providers

 16.     This i s one o fthe most important accountability mechanisms as it enables parents,
 individuals, and community-based organizations to holdtheir local service providers



                                              135

accountable for the delivery o f quality education services. Underneath the Oversight
Management Committees are the following: Boards o f Governors o f Secondary Schools,
School Management Committees and Parents' Associations o fprimary schools,
community-based management committees o fNFE and ECD Centers, and Management
Boards o fYouth Polytechnics.

17.     With 18,500 primaryschools spread acrossthe country, it is clearly impossible for
DE0staffto regularlymonitor theperformance o fstaffworking at primary and
secondary schools, as well as ECD andNFE centers. It i s therefore essential that existing
OversightManagement Committees are strengthened so that theyhave the capacity to
review and to report on the performance o f frontline service providers to DEBs. The
importance o f these Oversight Management Committees are highlighted inthe Sessional
Paper 2005 which outlines a number o f policies which will strengthen the capacity o f
Oversight Management Committees so that they can take a more active role inensuring
that children intheir community access quality education services.

(ii) Between District Education Boards and District Education Ofices

18.     Inthe SessionalPaper2005 special emphasishasbeenplacedontheroleofDEBS
inmonitoringtheperformanceofDEOs, andmorebroadly, education service provision
withineachdistrict. These policies buildon the success o freforms undertaken bythe
MOESTinrecent years to decentralize oversight and decision-making authority to DEBs
who are clearly better placedto manage the provision o f education services within each
district. Inorder to successfully implementthe KESSP this accountability mechanism
will be extremely important as it will ensure that DE0 staff are heldto account for their
performance.

(iii) Between the KESSP Secretariat and Division Directors

19.     An importantrole o fthe KESSP Secretariat will be to monitor the overall
implementationo f the KESSP and report this information on a regular basis to the five
Directors at the MOE. When relevant, this information will be shared by Directors with
their counterparts on Inter-Agency Coordinating Committees.

(iv) Between the Permanent Secretary and Government and development partners

20.     This horizontal accountability already exists as both, the MOE and development
partners, have realizedthe importance o f regular coordination and information sharing to
develop the SWAP and KESSP. Following the launch o fthe KESSP, this accountability
mechanism i s providing an opportunity for development partners, and sometimes senior
managers from other Government Ministries, to review the performance o f the M O E in
KESSP implementation.




                                            136

         Annex 9: FinancialManagementandDisbursementArrangements
                      KENYA: EducationSector SupportProject

A. FinancialManagementArrangements

Introduction

1.     The purpose o fthe program's financial management assessment i s to determine
whether the financial management systemis capable o fproducingtimely,
understandable, relevant, and reliable financial information that will allow the World
Bank, other donors and the Government to plan and implement the program, monitor
compliance with agreed procedures, and appraise progress towards its objectives. The
assessment was aimed at determining the program's readiness to adopt report-based
disbursements,the preferredmethod o fdisbursement under a SWAP.

2.     Inlinewithnormal SWAPinstitutionalarrangements, theprogramwill, as much
as possible, rely on mainstream Government financial management systems. It will also
buildon existing systems useduponduringthe implementationo fthe ongoing IDA-
financed FPESP. These systems are considered sufficiently capable o fproper financial
management. A summary o f the findings o f the financial management assessment i s as
follows.

CountryIssues

3.     The current Government that came into office inDecember 2002 has made a
commitment to strengthen the financial management and control environment inorder to
achieve economy, efficiency and effectiveness inthe use o fpublic funds. With the
support o f a number o f donor-assisted initiatives, including the IDA-funded Public Sector
Management Technical Assistance Project (PSMTAP), the Government is seekingto
rapidly enhance the financial accountability framework, particularly through
strengthening legislation relatedto public financial management andthe audit o fpublic
funds.

4.     A newCountry Assistance Strategy (CAS) was prepared inMay2004. The most
recent piece o f diagnostic work that provides an up to date critical assessment o f issues
that may impact on this operation at country level i s the Country IntegratedFiduciary
Assessment (CIFA). Both these documents have reviewed the Government's
performance since the last Country Financial Accountability Assessment (in2001) and
the previous CAS (in 1998). A recurringtheme is that policy changes agreed underpast
and ongoing projects have not beenimplemented consistently. Project implementation
has generally been slowed down by constraints inthe flow o fresources and limited
absorptive capacity, arising from bureaucratic processes inGovernment. A process i s
now underway a process to design a Joint Country Assistance Strategy for many
development partnersto use as their common framework to providing support to Kenya.




                                             137

 5.       There i s also now a system o f "rolling" and continuous country program review,
 andthe recent reports highlightthe Government's commitment to improvingportfolio
 performance. Agreement has beenreached on several key issues, some o f which have
 been applied inthe design o fthis operation. These include actions to: (i)   improve audit
 compliance; (ii)   monitor more closely project performanceby the MOF; and (iii)     improve
 the flow o fproject resources. Specifically, 6 ministries are now participating ina roll out
 o f a risk based internal audit approach; the MOF is conducting regular reviews o f World
 Bank finded projects with a strong focus on the achievement of results; andnew
 mechanisms for transferring resources to the local level with enhanced social
 accountability, as piloted inthe Free PrimaryEducation Program, have been extended to
 other sectors such as the ASAL program.

  Summaryof FinancialManagementAssessment

  (i)     Institutional and stafJing arrangements

  6.      The MOEwill be responsible for the overall financial management o fthe project.
 Project activities will, as far as possible, bebased on existing institutional arrangements,
  including the MOE's accounting andreporting systems. Matters requiringattention in
  order to ensure the establishment o freliable accounting and internal control systems
  include:

  (a) Financial management staffing at the head office. There is a need to enhance the
  technical capacity o fhead office financial management staff, particularly the filling o f
  vacant positions and the further training o fkey staff infinancial monitoring reporting.
  To addressthe capacity buildingneeds, there is an ongoing training program for key
  staff. This includes training of the financial management specialists staff (with the latest
  capacity buildingcarried out inAugust 2006, following training o f five staff on FMR's),
  investment program leaders and all senior staff o f the MOE. Selected financial
  management staffhave attended intensive training inMalawi.

  (b) Financialmanagement capacity. There are also ongoing initiatives to buildfinancial
  management capacity at school and district levels. Price Waterhouse Coopers was
  contracted to carry out extensive financial management training for school heads and
  district education officers under the FPESP. This exercise was completed as plannedin
  2004/05, and further capacity enhancement will be provided underthe KESSP.

  (io     Fundsflow and accountability arrangements

  7.      While it is intendedthat reliance be placed, as far as possible, on mainstream
  Governmentarrangements for the flow o f finds, there are several critical weaknesses that
  need to be addressed. These comprise:

  (a) Capacity o f district treasuries. There have beenmajor bottlenecks to the smooth flow
. o f finds and accountabilities, owing to capacity constraints and bureaucratic processes.
  To mitigate this constraint, arrangements have already beenput inplace for the direct
  crediting o f funds to school-managed bank accounts for the purchase o f instructional


                                                 138

materialsunder the FPESP. Under this arrangement, schools maintain simplified fund
accountability statements and submit monthly statements o f expenditure and fund balance
statements (supported by bank statements and reconciliations) to respective district
education offices for collation and submission to the MOE head office. Accounting
records are retained andmaintained at the school level.

(b) Simplification of accountingprocesses. Considerable successhas already been
achieved insimplifyingaccountingprocedures at the primaryschool levels. However,
findremittance andpayment processes shouldbe reviewedwith aview to improving
efficiency, effective control and timeliness. Key considerations inthe process will
include: (i)  realigning the role o f technical oversight functions from involvement in
routine transaction processing to conducting independent reviews; and (ii)     redefining the
role o f Internal Audit to that o f independently conducting institutional risk management
on an ongoing basis, monitoring compliance with laid downpolicies andprocedures, and
reviewingand recommending enhancement o f accounting and internal controls. The
M O E also needs to develop benchmark processing timelines that should thenbe adopted
and monitored.

(iii)    Risk management and internal control arrangements

8.       Inorderto ensureproperaccountabilityoffundsatthe schoollevel, ongoing
initiatives to develop financial monitoring capacity o f district education offices should be
expedited. Meanwhile, the capacity and role o fministerial oversight functions o f Internal
Audit andthe School Audit Unitshouldbereviewedand enhancedto achieve aproactive
riskmanagement function.

(iv)     Financial monitoring reporting

9.       Financial MonitoringReports (FMRs) are the preferredbasis o f reporting
disbursement under SWAP. This method i s already adopted by the FPESP. However,
there are a number of concerns relating to the new program:

(a) Collation o fmonthly accountabilities. Given the large number o f reporting entities, it
i s critical that the capacity o f reporting units to prepare comprehensive, yet simplified and
standardized, monthly statements o f expenditure andfundbalances be developed as soon
as possible. Underthis arrangement, it is expected that district education offices will
collate and submit information relating to their regions on a timely basis. National
spending unitswill report directly to the head office.

(b) FMRpreparationcapacity. MOEcapacityto prepare and submit quarterly FMRsis
assessedas weak, as heavy reliance i s placed on a few individuals. However, training i s
now underway for both financial management staff and the managers o f the investment
programs, and the M O E has successfully producedthe first FMR for the KESSP for the
period April to June 2006. The FMR for the period from July to September 2006 is
presentlyunder preparation, and the process o f its preparation i s beingused for additional
training purposes.



                                                 139

(v)     Independent audit arrangements

10.     UnderKenyan legislation, the responsibility to audit all Government funds and
activities i s vested inthe Kenya National Audit Office (KNAO), which i s mandated to
subcontract such services inthe event o f capacity or other constraints. Due consideration
will be taken ofthe KNAO's capacity and commitment to ensuring compliance with
Government and development partners' requirements for timely auditing and reporting.
While there has been significant improvement inthe office's ability to ensure timely
auditing and reporting o f donor-fundedprojects' audits, the audit reports of the Central
Government financial statements, including those o f MOE, continue to be delayed.
However, the audit reports for the FPESP are up to date and are considered satisfactory.

(vi)    Financial management manual

11.     The project's accounting and internal control procedures have been documented
inaFinancialManagement Manualfor the MOEandMOST. Themanualincorporates
MOF financial management guidelines, as well as guidelinesdeveloped bythe MOEST,
including those developed under the FPESP. The manuals havebeendrafted and
reviewed by IDA and other development partners participating inthe SWAP. Inaddition
to the MOE and MOST Manual, there i s another Financial Management Manual for
Secondary Schools, Colleges and Polytechnics, and also an existing Handbook o f
Financial Management Instructions for Primary Schools.

(vii)   Joint FinancingAgreement

12.     Development partners participating inthe SWAP pooled fund arrangement have
drafted the JFA (Annex 11)that spells out, among other matters, agreed procedures to be
adopted under the program including: (i)    institutional arrangements; (ii)
                                                                          procurement;
(iii)financial management and disbursements; (iv) interim and annual reporting; (v)
annual audit; and (vi) joint consultation, information, monitoring and evaluation
arrangements. The JFA has been signedby the Government, the World Bank, DFID and
UNICEF.




                                              140

(viii)  Financial management actionplan

13.     The outcome o fthis review is included ina financial management improvement
plan, comprising actions to be completedprior to credit effectiveness, and ongoing
actions to be followed up duringprogram implementation.

FinancialManagement Systems

(0      Internal controls andfinancial management guidelines

14.     Theproject's internal controls are based on the MOE's established accounting
andinternalcontrol systems and documented inthe Government financial operations
manual and guidelines. The manualhas beenreviewed, andnotable weaknesses have
been identified and addressed inthe project design and financial management actionplan.
These include: (i) weak capacity o f district treasuries; (ii) needfor simplification
                    the                                         the
o f accounting and find remittanceprocesses; and (iii)   the need to review the
effectiveness o f the internal audit function. Specifically, new mechanisms have been
developed for transferring resources directly to the school level; accountingprocedures
have been developed for use by local communities; and internal audit is beingreformed
along risk basedprinciples.

15.     The procedures usedby the MOEto maintainits records include the requirement
for cross references to supporting documentation inthe statement o f expenditure (SOE)
supporting schedules, inorder to facilitate the inspection o f these schedules andto
improve the maintenance o f accountingrecords. School level accounting and internal
control procedures are contained inseparate manuals. There i s a Financial Management
Manual for Secondary Schools, Colleges and Polytechnics, and a separate Handbook o f
Financial Management Instructions for Primary Schools (both developed with the support
o fFPESP consultants). The manuals describe the accounting system: the major
transaction cycles; funds flow processes; accountingrecords; supporting documents;
computer files and specific accounts inthe financial statements relating to the processing
o f transactions; list o f accounting codes used for group transactions (chart o f accounts);
andthe accountingprocesses from the initiationo f atransaction to its inclusioninto the
general ledger.

(io     Planning and budgeting

16.      Budgeting for the project has been undertaken centrally by the M O E and MOST,
inconsultationandwith extensive detailedinputsbythe respective implementing entities.
The budget is based on the MTEF. Proposed periodic reporting guidelines focus on
activity, cash flow andprocurement projections. The analysis andreview o fprogress
will be included inthe quarterly FMRs that will form the basis o frequests for
reimbursement o f funds.




                                              141

(iii)   Books of accounts and list of accounting codes

17.     The project's accounting records will bemaintained on the existing Government's
systems. The codes related to the project have been integratedinthe MOE's Chart o f
Accounts, which will match the classification o f financial statements. The project's
Financial Management Manualprovides specific guidelines regardingprocedures to be
adopted for the maintenance o f accountingrecords, including proper custody o f
accountable documents.

(iv)    Internal audit

18.     Owingto lack o fclarity regarding its purpose, internal audit hadpreviously been
involved inroutine transaction processing functions, thereby underminingits intended
purpose o fproviding independent oversight o f accounting and internal control processes.
There are ongoing initiatives to realign its functioning along best practice guidelines
issued bythe International Institute of Internal Auditors, including implementationo f
systems compliance andrisk-based approach. Members o f a Ministerial Audit
Committee have already been appointed bythe Permanent Secretary, MOE, with the
independent Inspector o f State Corporations beingrepresented, and a risk-based internal
audit plandeveloped. Planning i s also underwayto involve universities inindependent,
periodic internal audits. The roll out planfor developing the risk-based internal audit
approach has beenreviewed bythe Bank and by two external authorities, and has been
found to be acceptable. Further consultations are taking place with the Government's
Internal Auditor General andhis team at the M O E to agree upon implementation
arrangements. Finally, the MOEwill continue to use an Independent Administrator to
ensure full accountability andprobity at the Kenya Instituteo f Education for the
successful instruction materials program, andspecifically for the transparent and
competitive selection o fpublishers for incorporationo fbooks into the approved list.

(v)     External audit

19.      Under Kenyan legislation, the responsibilityto audit all Government funds and
activities is vested inthe KNAO, which i s mandated to subcontract such services inthe
event o f capacity or other constraints. While there has beensignificant improvement in
the office's ability to ensure timely auditing and reporting o fdonor-funded projects'
audits, Central Government financial statements' audit reports, including those o f MOE,
continue to be delayed. The Government will ensure that for each fiscal year an audit o f
the KESSP is undertakeninaccordance with existingauditing standards, andthat the
report i s shared with development partners within six months following the end o f the
financial year. The audit report will include an audit o f all program bank accounts and
will specifically audit the pooled fund eligible expenditures. Private sector audit funds
will be contracted to supplement KNAO's capacity ifnecessary. The JFA signatories
mayrequest a performancerelated audit ifconsidered appropriate.




                                              142

(vi)   Reporting and monitoring

20.    The primarycontents o f quarterly FMRs comprise:

a) Financial Reports
    Sources and Uses o f Fundsby FundingSource
    Uses o f FundsbyProject Activity/Component
b) PhysicalProgress (Output Monitoring) Report
c) Procurement Report

21.    FMRs will cover all project activities, including Government counterpart funding,
resources providedbypartners not participating inthe SWAP arrangement, and non-cash
contributions wherever reasonably quantifiable. As FMRswill form the basis o fperiodic
fundingdisbursements by development partners, they will include a detailed report o f
projected cash requirements for each ensuing6 month-period supported by: (i)      a
procurement progress report and related cash flow projection; and (ii)    a fundbalance
statement supported bybank balance certificates and reconciliation statements. The first
FMRfor the KESSP(for the periodAprilto June 2006) has already been successfully
producedby the MOE.

(vii)   Financia1statements

22.     MOEfinancial statements will beprepared inaccordance with International
Public Sector Accounting Standards (which inter alia includes the application o f the cash
basis o frecognition o f transactions). Indicative formats o f financial statements havebeen
agreed duringnegotiations (with the first FMRproduced ina manner satisfactory to the
Association). Project financial statements will include:

1) Statement of sources and uses of fundskash receipts andpayments for each fbnded
    period which recognizes all cash receipts, cash payments and cash balances controlled
    bythe entity; and separately identifies payments bythirdparties onbehalf o fthe
    entity.

2) Accounting policies adopted with explanatory notes. The explanatory notes should
    be presented ina systematic manner with items on the Statement of Cash Receipts
    and Payments being cross referenced to any related information inthe notes for each
    fundedperiod. Examples o fthis information include a summary o ffixed assetsby
    category o f assets, and a summary of SOE Withdrawal Schedule, listing individual
    withdrawal applications.

3) Management assertion that project funds have been expended inaccordance with the
    intended purposes as specified inthe relevant fundingagreements for each funded
    period.

23.     IDAfundingagreements require the submission ofaudited financial statements,
taking into consideration the 2003 Audit Policy Guidelines o fthe World Bank, within six



                                             143

months after the year-end. A similar agreement with development partners i s included in
the JFA. The FMRs will be shared with at least all partners inthe JFA.

(viii) Supervisionplan

24.    Joint supervision missions by development partners will be conducted at least
every six months, once inOctober for the annualjoint review and once inMarcWApril at
the budget workshop. Eachmission's objectives will ensure that strong financial
management systems are maintained for the project throughout its lifespan. Inaddition,
SOEreviewswill be carried out regularly to ensure that expenditures incurredbythe
project remain eligible.

B. DisbursementArrangements

25.     Requests for disbursement by development partners for activities to be financed
from pooled funds will be made on the basis o f approved work plans and cash flow
projections for eligible expenditures (report-based disbursements). Development partners
will make advance disbursements inagreedproportions from the proceeds o frespective
credits or grants into the project Special Account to expedite project implementation.
Advances will be usedby the Borrower to fund eligible project expenditures.

26.     Replenishment of funds to the Special Account will be made upon evidence o f
satisfactory utilization o f the advance, reflected inthe FMR. Replenishmentapplications
will be requiredto be submitted at least six monthly. However, under the JFA, it is
expected that disbursements will be made three times a year, notably inJuly, November
and February. Inline with the JFA, the Bank has been appointed as the first "contact
point" for reviewing FMRs and advising on disbursements. Even inadvance o f the
approval of IDA support to the KESSP, the World Bank has already been facilitating the
FMRreviewprocess, working with both, the other pooling development partners andthe
MOE.

27.     Another acceptable method o fwithdrawing funds under this arrangement is the
direct payment method, involvingdirect payments to suppliers for works, goods and
services uponthe Borrower's request. Payments may also be made to a commercial bank
for expenditures against pre-agreed special commitments. Respective development
partners' disbursement letters will stipulate the minimumapplication value for direct
payment and special commitment procedures.

Remedies

28.     Ifineligibleexpenditures are found to havebeenmadefromSpecial Accounts, the
Borrower will be obligated to refindthe full amount o fthe expenditure. Such a refund
will be indirect proportion to respective partners' contributions inthe case o fpooled
financing, as providedinthe JFA. If     the Special Accounts remaininactive for more than
six months, development partners may reduce the amount advanced. Development
partners will also have the right, reflected inrespective funding agreements, to suspend



                                               144

 disbursement o f finds ifreportingrequirements are not complied with. So far there are
 three external signatories to the JFA (IDA, DFID andUNICEF). DFID and IDA are fully
 agreed onprocedures. Consultations continue with UNCIEF (so far contributing
 US$250,000 to the pooled fund) onthe procedures. IfUNICEF does not accept the
 procedures thenits fundingwould be managed in a manner consistent with boththe JFA
 and its own requirements.

                         Annex 10: ProcurementArrangements
                       KENYA:EducationSector Support Project

Procurementenvironment

1.     Kenya's first National Procurement Law (The Public Procurement and Disposal
Act) was passed by Parliament inOctober 2005. The Act is not yet effective, as the
supporting Regulations (which are necessary for the effective implementationo fthe Act)
have not yet been approved. However, the regulations have been drafted by the Public
Procurement Department (PPD) o fMOF and discussed widely. It is expected that the
Act will be effective before the end o f 2006. Since 2001,the Public Procurement
Regulations (Regulations 200l),    issuedbythe Minister o fFinance, havebeen enforced.
The Regulations (2001) apply to procurement by public entities (including, inter alia,
central government departments andstate corporations). These year 2001 Regulations
will ceaseto be usedonce the new Act is effective.

2.      Public Procurement inKenya i s recognized as a very important process via which
over 70 percent o fpublic funds (excluding staff emoluments, debt servicing and other
statutory payments) are spent. A major challenge inKenya i s to put inplace sound and
efficient public procurement systems that ensure value for money, efficiency inservice
deliveryandtransparency, including providing equal opportunity to the bidding
community. It i s recognized that corruption inpublic procurement i s a major issue in
Kenya, as it i s very erosive o fpublic funds intended for public good and its resultant
economic growth. So while the new Regulations offer a great opportunity to achieve
value for money, enhance transparency, provide equal opportunity to all bidders, and
reduce corruption inpublic procurement, this has not happened due to weak capacity o f
procurement staff across the board, and also weak enforcement o f compliance to the
Regulations. The Procurement Act will face the same challenges when effective. For
these reasons, the Government i s now emphasizing capacity building and the
development o f additional materials for line Ministries and all agencies responsible for
procurement activities.

ProcurementLegal andRegulatoryFramework

3.      CountryProcurementAssessment Reports(CPAR) andEducationSector
ProcurementAssessment Report(ESPAR). The last Kenya Country Procurement
Assessment Review (CPAR) was conducted in 1997. The CPAR recommended
procurement reforms. In 1998 the Government applied and received from the World
Bank an IDF grant with which the Government started a procurement reform program.
The main outcomes o f the reform program was: (i)      establishment and gazettement in
March 2001 o f the Public Procurement Regulations (Regulations 2001), which govern all
public procuring entities; and (ii) training o f over 150public officials inpublic
procurement. But it appears that most o fthose trained are either retired and/or no longer
inprocurement positions.




                                              148

Scope of Procurement

4.      The implementationofKESSP entails procurement o f

(i)     Goods - vehicles, textbooks, teaching andlearning materials, furniture, school,
        laboratory, office and field equipment, computers and accessories, etc.

(ii)    Works - improvement o f school facilities, construction o fnew buildings,
        classrooms, toilets, water facilities, boundarywalls andfences, maintenance and
        repair o f buildings,technical services for maintenance o f equipment.

(iii)   Consulting services - in-service training o f teachers, principals and members o f
        school committees, monitoring and evaluation, engineering services, procurement
        services, financial management and audit, andvarious types o f studies.

ProcurementImplementationArrangements

5.      The implementationo f the KESSP will be carried out through 23 separate
investment programs and ina decentralized set-up. The MOE, Districts, Semi
Autonomous Government Agencies (SAGAS) under the MOE, (i.e. the Kenya Institute o f
Education-KIE, the Teachers' Service Commission-TSC, the Kenya Education Staff
Institute-KESI, Public Universities and the Kenya National Examinations Council-
KNEC), polytechnics and secondary schools, and primary schools will independently
mange their procurement usingtheir budgetaryallocations under the program. In
summary, procurement for the implementationo fthe program will be procured at three
levels; i.e. (i)
               school and community; (ii) and (iii)
                                             district;       national level (MOE and
SAGAs). The highest decision making authority for procurement at the school and
community level is the School Management Committee (SMC); at the district level the
District Tender Committee; at MOE, the Ministerial Tender Committee; and at SAGA,
the SAGA Tender Committee. Except for schools, other agencies are expected to have a
Procurement Unit with qualified staff to carry out procurement tasks. The existing
procurement capacity needs to be strengthenedat all levels. The MOEhas developed a
procurement capacity buildingplanfor KESSP implementing entities. The planwill be
embedded inthe revised Procurement Procedures Manuals. The MOE has also drawn up
an actionplanfor the monitoring and evaluation o f the implementationo fthe
procurement capacity buildingplan. The responsibilities o fthe KESSP implementing
entities inthe supervision, monitoring and evaluation o fthe capacity buildingaction will
be definedinthe revised Procurement Procedures Manuals.

6.      At the school level, there has already been a successful program o fdecentralized
procurement for instructional materials usingstrong community involvement, with
guidelinesand well-developed manuals, transparent accountability, andregular
monitoring. Inaddition, under the KESSP there will be decentralized procurement for
the school infrastructure improvement program and the constructiono f new schools, as
well as for small grants for special needs education. Safeguards include operational




                                               149

guidelines satisfactory to the Association issuedby the MOE; regular inspection and
supervision; strict enforcement o f guidelines; and continued, strong social accountability.

        ProcurementPlans

7.      M O E Headquarters, Provincial and District Offices, Semi-autonomous Agencies
(including Public Universities), Secondary Schools, Colleges andPolytechnics (all
referredto as Procuring Entities)will be required to prepare their procurementplans
consistent with their Annual Workplans, Government budget cycle and approved budget
allocations. MOE `s first procurementplanwill be for years 1and 2 covering all
contracts for goods and services, including selection o f any technical assistance
personnel. Eachprocurement plan should be prepared as part o fbudget preparation
process, finalized by July 31yearly, and cleared with the M O E Procurement Unit by
August 31yearly. An approvedprocurement planshould be atrigger for release o ffbnds
to the Entity. The procurement planwill take into account any contracts that may spill
over from a previous year. M O E will provide development partners, for their review, a
draft annual procurement plan inaccordance with the format usedinthe KESSP work
planandprocurementprofile for 2005/06.

8.      The procurement plans will be posted inthe MOE's database, and the MOE's
plan will be postedinthe World Bank's external website. The "Actual" column o f the
procurement planwill be updated regularly so as to reflect progress made on each
contract package, to include any new packages that were not foreseen at the time o f
preparing the plan andto indicate any packages no longer needed. The Procurement Unit
o fM O Ewill at the end o f each fiscal year review the performance o f the execution o f
these plans, so as to determine the quantityo fthe plan achieved each year. Low
performance could mean that procurement i s holding hostage budget execution and
service delivery.

        Procurementcompliancereviews

9.      The partners have agreedthat audit will be carried out as per the system and
procedures o f the Government, and as per the provisions inthe JFA. Procurement
compliance reviews14will be the responsibilityo fthe M O E Procurement Unit andthe
national oversight body. Given the staffing constraints o f the MOE Procurement Unit,
the MOEProcurement Unit should out-source this task to consulting findprivate sector,
so that the Unit i s left with the responsibilityo f supervising the consultants carrying out
the reviews. The reviews do not needto cover prior review contracts except for
perfoxmance o f signedcontracts. The review reports will include recommendations for
actions to be taken incase o f non-compliancewith the procurement procedures and plans.
The reviewswill be conducted at a frequency to be agreed betweenthe MOE and
participatingpartners. The procurement compliance reviews should cover at least 15
percent o f all contracts under the program. Incase o f mis-procurement inthe program,


14 These reviews are not strictly audits but reviews of selected contracts to see the extent to which they
    have complied with agreed procurement procedures. These reviews also include procurement record
    keeping and existing capacity that support procurement function.


                                                   150

the development partners reserve the rightto take corrective measures, inaccordance
with their respective policies, including suspending further disbursements to the KESSP
or reclaiming funds already disbursed. The amount so refunded would be inproportion
to the amount contributed by each development partner.


        APPLICABLE PROCUREMENT PROCEDURES

        BankGuidelinesand StandardBiddingDocuments

10.     Procurement o f goods, civil works and employment o f consultants will be subject
to the provisions o f the World BankProcurement Guidelines, i.e. Guidelines:
Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (May 2004) (for goods andworks), and
Guidelines: Selection and Employment o f Consultants by World Bank Borrowers (May
2004), and to current national public procurement procedures acceptable to the
Association and as described below. Bank's Standard BidDocuments (SBD) and
procedures will be used for all International Competitive Bidding(ICB). Bank's
Standard Request for Proposals (RFP) andprocedures will be used for selecting
consultants on basis o f Quality and Cost Based Selection (QCBS). The use o f World
Bank Procurement Guidelines andProcedures for all International Competitive Bidding
Procurement, usingpooled funding, has been accepted by the Government andDFID,
which are signatory to the JFA. Where future donors do not subscribe to the JFA, the
contracts financed by them could be earmarked for financing by a particular donor using
its own rules andprocedures under parallel co-financing.

        Nationalprocurementprocedures

11.     The nationalprocurementprocedures are established inthe Government Public
Procurement Regulations o f 2001 and inthe Public Procurement and Disposal Act o f
2005, but the latter i s not yet effective. The Act will be effective as soon as the
Regulations are approved. The Bank reviewed the Public Procurement Regulations o f
2001 and found them acceptable except for the provision that allows direct contracting by
a threshold as opposed to by exception as provided inthe Act.

12.     MOEhas producedProcurement Procedures Manuals (referred to as Manuals)
inconsultationwith the stakeholders, for (a) MOEHeadquarters, ProvincialandDistricts
Offices; (b) Semi-Autonomous Government Agencies, including public Universities
(SAGAS); (c) for Secondary Schools, Colleges and Polytechnics; and (d) Primary
Schools. The Manuals (including supplementary school procurement management
handbooks) lay down the institutional arrangements for managing procurement processes
and making decisions, and procedures for procuring goods and services under each
KESSP implementing entity. The procurement procedures inthe Manuals have been
agreed inprinciple with partners participating infinancing the KESSP through the pooled
fundingarrangement. The guidelines contained inthe Manuals andHandbooks, as well
as any future modifications thereof, will be issued to the implementingentities.




                                              151

13.     The Manuals havebeen reviewed by the Bank andneed some revisionsto clarify
the applicable procurementprocedures. Some information needs to be corrected and
some additional information is requiredto strengthen capacity to conduct procurement
activities at all levels. These Manuals also need to be cleared with the Public
Procurement Department to ensure they are aligned with the current procurement
regulations, and with the emerging procurement legal and institutional framework. They
do not describe insufficient detail the various procurement tasks, identifyingthe
personnel responsible for each task, including the roles o fthe Procurement Units. They
do not specify the role o f the MOEProcurement Unit inapproving procurementplans,
monitoring their implementation(including monitoring compliance with procurement
regulations) and coachingprocurement staff at lower levels. These deficiencies will be
addressedby revising the Manuals to ensure the programprocurement i s carried out inan
efficient manner. The revision o f the manuals, acceptable to the Association i s a
condition o f effectiveness.

14.      International CompetitiveBidding (ICB) will be usedfor contracts above US$1
million for works, and above US$250,000 for goods. Pre-qualificationo f contractors will
be used only for large contracts over US$lO million, and incases where special expertise
i s required.

15.      For all contracts below ICB and QCBS thresholds, National Competitive bidding
(NCB), Shopping, Community participation and Direct Contracting will be used
following the national procedures inthe Procurement Regulations o f 2001 or the Public
Procurement and Disposal Act of 2005 from the date it i s made effective, and any
Regulations issued to guide such procurement.

16.     Quality-and Cost-BasedSelection (QCBS). Except as detailed below, consulting
services will be selected through this method. Shortlists for contracts estimated to cost
less than US$200,000 may consist o fnational firms andNGOs, where available.

17.      Least Cost Selection (LCS) may be used for assignments o f a standard androutine
nature for auditing and engineering services o fno complex nature.

 18.     Selection based on Consultants Qualijkations (CQ,), Single-Source Selection and
Selectionof Individual Consultants, may be usedfor assignments estimated to be below
US$50,000

         Prior Review Thresholds

 19.     The following will be subject to prior review by the World Bank: (i) contracts
                                                                              all
for goods over US$150,000; (ii)   all contracts for works over US$250,000; (iii) all
consultant services contracts for firms over US$100,000; and (iv) all consultant services
contracts for individuals over US$50,000. The thresholds are summarized below in
Table A.




                                             152

20.     All other contracts will be subject to post-review bythe Bank and/or other
development partners, after consultations on respective responsibilities for procurement
review activities. MOE and all participating agencies will maintain accurate records o f
all procurement activities and documents relatedto the Program. The procurement files
will be maintained for reviewby the Bank's supervisionmissions and independent
auditing, including consolidating procurement activities into Quarterly and Annual
Progress Reports.

        Frequencyof ProcurementSupervision

21.     Banksupervisionmissionswillbecarried out three times inthe first year (at the
joint reviews inOctober and April, plus at an additional time), and every 6 months
thereafter. Two ex-post procurement review audits will be done duringthe first year, and
from thereon at least once a year.

        Assessment of ProcurementCapacity andRisk

22.     An Education Sector Procurement Assessment Review(ESPAR) o fexisting
procurement procedures was carried out inconjunction with other development partners.
The existingbasic framework o frules andprocedures inKenyarequires open tenders
(being open to all bidderswithout discrimination), the use o f non-discriminatory tender
documents, public bidopening andthe selection o f the most advantageous tender taking
all factors (preferably pre-disclosed) into consideration. The review also found that while
the regulationsprovide for a transparent procurement system, problems are emerging
with the implementationo fthese rules. Theproblems emanate from a weak institutional
arrangement for procurement oversight, absence o f capacity inpublic procurement
procedures for civil works contracts by communities, and lack o f capacity ininternational
procurement procedures o f the procurement staff inthe MOE.

23.     The MOEhas already developed (under the ongoing FPESP) comprehensive
guidelines for the procurement o f instructional materials at the community level. School
Management Committees (which are incharge o f implementation o f the procurement o f
instructionalmaterials) have been trained on the guidelines and are successfully
 following the procedures prescribedinthe guidelines.

24.     Based on the findings o f the ESPAR and the assessment o f the existing
procurement capacity, it has been concludedthat the existing system would be acceptable
 for ensuringadequate transparency only whenthe measures and conditions o f Credit
Effectiveness are implemented. Additional areas for procurement capacity strengthening
have been identified inthe "Education Sector Governance andAccountability Action
Plan" (Annex 2).

 25.    A further assessmentbyBank staffhas found that keysenior positions o f
Procurement Staff inMOE are vacant, andthat some M O E procurement staffhave not
beenretrainedfor some time. Procurement hnctions are also difhsed into the activities
 o ftechnical and general staff inthe MOE, with little work being carried out bythe



                                             153

procurement staff themselves. This diffusion has (i) difficulties inprocurement
                                                        caused
decisions, given lack o fprocurement advice from a competent procurement specialist,
and (ii)eroded the efficiency o f the Procurement Unit and denied them the opportunity to
learn andbe innovative and efficient as a Unit. The Procurement Procedures Manuals
have been reviewed and need revisions to meet the users' needs, as described in
paragraph 13 above.

26.    Theprocurement risk is assessedas "substantial" given: (i) lack of adequate
                                                                      the
staffing inMOEHeadquarters and other institutions, with key senior positions o f
Procurement Staff vacant inM O E andpossibly also inother institutions; (ii)      most o fthe
existing procurement staff lack sufficient procurement experience inthe use o f rigorous
national procedures and international procurement procedures; (iii)    there i s inadequate
capacity inthe MOE for supervising civil works contracts andprograms; (iv) the
procurement process inthe MOE is generally inefficient, givenprocurement fimctions are
diffused into the activities o f technical and general staff, many o fwhom have no
technical knowledge o f procurement; (v) the weak institutional arrangement for
procurement oversight at bothnational andMOE levels; and (vi) weaknesses inthe
Procurement Procedures Manuals prepared by the MOE.

27.     Inorderto mitigatethis risk, MOEshouldtake the following specific measures
prior to credit effectiveness:

(i)     The MOE shouldfzll vacantpositions of Head of theprocurement unit and
procurement offzcer. These positions are already identified on the structure o fthe staff
positions o f the Procurement Unit o f MOE.

(ii)    MOE should recruit a TechnicalAssistanceProcurement Specialist to assist the
Procurement Unit coach the procurement staff o f the M O E Procurement Unit in
procurementplanning, capacitybuildingactivities, monitoring compliance with
procurement regulations, andinprocurement processing under rigorous national and
international procurement procedures.

(iii)   M O E should revise the Procurement Procedures Manuals to provide clarity on
the applicableprocurement procedures and to strengthen the capacity to conduct
procurement activities at all levels, the M O Ewill revise.

28.     The other measures that will mitigate the substantial risk are:

(i)     The MOE should ensure that procurement units o f implementing entities are
staffed with adequate numbers o f qualified staff, and that training is carried out to update
staff skills inprocurement. The procurement capacity buildingalready prepared should
be implemented as efficiently as possible inorder to bringinto the program the required
gains. Inaddition, it is expected that M O Ewill retainprocurement staff

(ii)    MOE should require implementing agencies to prepare procurement plans, inan
adequate and timely manner.



                                               154

(iii) UseshouldbemadeofWorldBankGuidelinesforallICBandQCBScontracts,
and their prior review by Bank staff will ensure that large contracts are carried out in
accordance with agreedprocedures andprocurement plans. Because o fpost reviews by
Bank staff, and frequent Bank supervision missions, procurement staff inM O E are
expected to comply with agreed procurement procedures to avoid the danger o fmis-
procurement beingdeclared.

(iv)   M O E should take action on recommendations from financial audits (these audits
will include procurement transactions), the procurement compliance reviews byMOE
and ex-post procurement reviews by the Bank. Such actions will help enforce
compliance with the procurement procedures andplans.

29.     Otherrecommendations for improving procurement include:

(i)     Operationalize the Procurement Oversight Authority byproviding it with
adequate staffing, and all necessary logistics.

(ii)    Finalize and publishthe regulations, including rolling out a training planon the
application o fthe new Procurement Act.

(iii) StrengthentheoversightroleoftheProcurementOversightAuthorityin
monitoring compliance with the procurement Act, and contract management, including
sound record-keepingsystems that will permit linksbetweenprocurement and
expenditure files, to support effective management o fthe contract process, especially
with respect to contract variations andmulti-yearpayments.

(iv)    Use procurement plans inthe preparationo f MTEF and annual budgets.

(v)     Ensure the roll out o fthe IFMIS, with the urgent operationalization o f the general
        ledgermodule.

(vi)    Place greater emphasis on risk management inthe internal audit process.

       (hcreinaftmn f m dto as KESSP) (2005-2010)     IS between:

       International Development Association (IDA), Dcpwtment for International Development
       (DFID),anyotherpoolingpartnerhereinafterr e f e d to as "DevelopmentPamere"

                          Annex 13: Economic and Financial Analysis
                          KENYA: Education Sector Support Project

1.       One o f the principle challenges facing the Kenyan education systemi s to improve
its overall quality by increasingthe percentage o f children completing eight years o f
primaryeducation, and also to improve access to secondary education. Additional
mobilization o fresources will help inreaching the goal but has the potential to impact the
entire Government budget. This section examines the fiscal impact o f expanding the
education system usinga model o f education finan~e'~.               The principal questionthat it
seeks to answer i s whether the policy options are financially feasible (and sustainable), so
that recurrent expenditure could be financed with internal resources with minimumneed
for external financingI6.

2.       The process for estimating the fiscal impact o f reform i s simple and i s done in
three steps. First, targets are set for quality, access andresources for the education sector.
Second, the education finance model i s usedto determine the cost o f additional resources
required to meet the targets. Finally, the impact o f additional cost on Government budget
i s estimated andthe financing gap identified.

3.       Simulation model: The model simulates the primary" and secondary education
sectors. While secondary technical/vocational education i s offered, the impact o fhigher
enrollments on these sectors has beenconsidered as part o f total secondary general
expenditure. It i s expected that the demandfor secondary education will rise sharply
over the period requiring substantial internal resources. Projections are made (until2010)
for estimating sustainability under the targets delineated inthe KESSP document and the
model i s extended till 2015 to calculate the costs related to attaining the goals outlined in
the FTI, usinginformation available inthe year 2002/03 as baseline data, and the
hypothesis outlined inthe KESSP document regarding the flow o f students through the
pre-university school cycles.

4.        Resources inthe model are determinedbased on the expected growth o f the
economy, total Government budget and the share o f education inthe budget.
Expenditurespertaining to reforms are determinedusing data on enrollment, efficiency
parameters such as repetition, dropouts and survival rates, and the rate o f transition
between education cycles. Cost variables include teacher salaries, non-salary spending
on materials and overheads, and transfers such as scholarships andcost o f providing
meals to students. Difference betweenthe cost o f expansion and the budget envelope
provides the financing gap for each sub sector.




 15 This work draws on the Public Expenditure Review done by the Ministry of Education and the teacher
     requirement model, which provides the data and assumptions outlined in the scenarios considered in
     this analysis. The results obtained from those scenarios (enrollments, resource requirements etc.) have
     been used inestimating the financial sustainability of the education program.
16 Investment expenditures are assumed to be co-financed with donor agencies.
17 Primary education constitutes eight years of education inKenya.


                                                    178

Variables and Assumptions

5.       Estimating the budget envelopefor education. The simulations are done under
the assumption that the economy will grow at an average rate o f 3.5 percent (IMF
projections) over the planning period, 2003-2010. The Government budget as a share o f
GDPis assumed to remainconstant at 6.2 (estimate for 2003/04) percent, while the share
o f education recurrent budget inGovernment recurrent budgeti s assumed to remain
constant at 33.1 percent. Flexibility ineducation finance comes from a decline in
primary school age population that will allow a reduction inthe share o fprimary
education inthe total recurrent education budget (though not falling below 55 percent o f
the total), thereby simultaneously enabling a transfer o f resources to secondary education
over the same period.

6.       Targetsfor studentsflow: Specific targets have been set inthe model for access
and efficiency o f education. These targets are set for all scenarios and are delineated in
Table 1. Primary education sector enrollments change as a result o f variation inschool-
age population (that increase by 2.9 percent in2010 and then decline by 3.2 percent in
2015) and improvement inprimary completion rate from 64 percent in2003 to 100
percent in2010, as per the KESSP objectives. With a target o f 70 percent transition from
primary into secondary (by 2010), enrollments are expected to increase significantly in
the secondary sub sector. Enrollment loss, consequently, is assumedto occur bothwithin
and between education cycles through dropouts".            Consistent with high survival rates
(dueto hightargeted completion rate), repetitionrates are assumedto come downto 5
percent inprimary edu~ation'~.Finally, the proportion o f students opting for private
schools inthe first grade o f primaryi s assumed to remain at 4 percent duringthe entire
reform period while private share i s expected to rise from 8 percent to 13.2 percent.
Overall, these access andinternal efficiency assumptions taken together will result in 124
percent more studentsinsecondary by 2010 and 146percent more insecondary by 2015
when compared to those in2005/06 (with a transition rate o f47 percent2').

7.       Changes in service deliveryparameters. These targets, shown inTable 2, will be
reached through improvement inthe values of parameters that determine the cost-
effectiveness o f services deliveredby the education system. Inaddition to internal
efficiency parameters2', gains inefficiency are also expected to come through the
deployment o fmore polyvalent teachers that have the potential to increase the average
number o f weekly hours spent on teaching insecondary education. Teacher salaries in
Kenya are projectedto grow annually at 3.5 percent (at the same rate as the economy)22.

8.       Three scenarios are examined for the two sub sectors o f education23and are
described indetail below:

 1. Baseline Scenario. The first scenario maintainsthe current values o fthe service
    delivery parameters and estimates the cost o freaching the target completion rate in
    primary, and the transition rate to secondary education as outlined inTable 124.Even
    though the education targets are attained inthis scenario, costs remain highbecause
    o fhighrepetition rates, and low level o f teachers' utilization that result ina larger
    number of students ineach cycle o f education.

 2. Efficiency Scenario. The second scenario seeks improvement inefficiency (in
    addition to access) o f the system by lowering the repetition rate to 5 percent in
    primary education25and raising class-size to 45 inbothprimary and secondary
    education.




 21 Table 2 describes the target values o f service delivery parameters (not efficiency) and hence, there is
    no difference inthe target values between Baseline and Efficiency scenarios.
 22 All projections are done inconstant Kenyan shillings.
 23 For primaryeducation, only the baseline and efficiency scenarios have been simulated.
 24 The primary completion rate is defined as the percentage o f new first grade enrollees completing the
    eighth grade.
 25 Repetition rates are assumed to decline gradually over the projection period.         Reported rates for
    secondary education are already low.


                                                    180

    3. Utilization Scenario. Inaddition to the gains inefficiency envisaged inthe second
          scenario, additional efficiency gains through optimal use o fteachinghours are
          modeled inthe third scenario that aims to increase the weekly number o fhours spent
          on teaching insecondary education through the use o fpolyvalent teachers.

    4. Share o fprivate enrollments. It i s envisaged inthe KESSP document that a
          significant portion o f new enrollments will go into private and/or community
          supported schools andwhere the cost o f educating them will not beborne by the
          public sector. The target for private secondary enrollments is at 13.2 percent o f new
          enrollees.

    9.         Cost of classroom construction: An expansion o fthe education system will
    require a substantial amount o fconstructionrelated to schools. Projectionsindicate a
    need for small number o f classrooms inprimary educationbut a much largerrequirement
    for secondary education sub sectors. The total capital cost o fproviding filly equipped
    additional classrooms at all levels o f education has not been estimated.

    10.        The evolutiono f student flows underdifferent scenarios is given inCharts 1and
    3, while the adequacy o f public financing for a given education sub sector in a given year
    (expressed by the percentage o frecurrent expenditure for an education sub sector that can

be financedby domestic recurrent budget for that sub sector duringthe same year26)
under different scenarios i s illustrated inCharts 2 and 4. Inthe following, the results o f
the simulation exercise are described for each education sub sector.

11.     Primary education: Demographic trends inChart 1indicate an increase in
primary education student-population till 2010 and then a decline till 2015. It is clear
from Chart 2, that ifthe present level o f financing i s maintained, the sub sector will be
able to finance its projected spendingbetween2006 and2010, but will then enjoy a
growing surplusover the plan subsequent period (baseline scenario). An increase in
internal efficiency (efficiency scenario) through a reductioninrepetition rates combined
with demographic decline results ina reductioninstudent enrollments that fall from 7.4
million in2006 to 7.3 million in2015.

12.      With no change inthe number o fteachers until2010 andnegotiatedwage
increases, simulations indicate that primaryeducation spendingwill be sustainable over
the plan period andgains inefficiency will result ina risingsurplus as enrollments
decline due to the decline inrepetition rates and school-age population. The rise in
internal efficiency results ina higher level o f surplus incomparison to the baseline
scenario. Additional simulations indicate that it may be possible to transfer resources
towards secondary education, with lower allocation for primary education, especially
after 2010 when student enrollments decline inthe sub sector.

13.        Inconclusion, underboththe KSSPandFTItargets27(primary completionrate of
100percent), the primary sub sector i s fiscally sustainable (underboththe Efficiency and
Baseline scenarios) and, infuture, will require less than its current share o f resources in
the education budget.

14.        Secondary education: Total enrollments insecondary education, as depictedin
Chart 3, are determinedby the interplay between demographic trends, improvement in
primary education's internal efficiency, and the reduction inrepetition rates insecondary.
Under the baseline scenario, total enrollments insecondary will reach 1.3 million in2010
andthenrise to 1.5 million in2015 due to the increase inschool-age population.
Efficiency improvements inprimaryhave the potential to raise the number o f eligible
primarystudentsfor entry into secondary. Ifthe transitionrateremains at 47 percent, the
number o f students insecondary education will increase to 1.33 millionby 2010 and 1.56
millionby 2015 due to decline inrepetition rate inprimary education. A transition rate
o f 70 percent, on the other hand, will raise the number o f secondary students to 1.88
million and2.29 million inrespective years.

15.        Inthis analysis, the numbers o fstudents insecondary education simulationshave
been estimated after accounting for efficiency improvement inthe primary education sub
sector, but not inthe secondary education. This allows the use o f a constant enrollment
trend for the two scenarios andisolates the fiscal impact o f eachpolicy decision.



27 It is important to note that the country may not achieve all the targets outlined in the fast track
      initiative by 2015 (especially fiscal targets), but it will be able to attain most o f the educational targets.

16.      Interms ofthe fiscal effect, it is clear from Chart 4, that the secondary education
sub sector system will be sustainable between2006 and 2010 under current (baseline)
values o f service delivery parameters and structure o f financing only because the
numbers o fteachers remain constant (the student-teacher ratio consequently rises), as
indicated inthe KESSP document. After 2010, however, significant deteriorationin
sustainability occurs due to the large increase inthe number o fnew teachers recruited.
Efficiency improvements through a larger class size (efficiency scenario) helps to
improve sustainability while the increase inthe actual number o f hours worked
(utilization scenario) through a deployment o f olyvalent teachers has a significant
impact on the magnitude o f the financing gap'!           It should, however, be notedthat despite
improvements inefficiency andutilizationo f resource^'^, the sub sector remains in
deficit. Sustainability, however, could bereached ifthe percentage o f new students
opting for private educationincrease from 8 to 13.2 percent (last scenario).

17.      Inconclusion, simulationsindicatethatthe Governmentcanattainits target of70
percent transition rate betweenprimary and secondary education by 2008 under the
following mix o f education policies:

         It shouldprovide incentivesto the private sector to play an increasingly important
         role inthe provision o f secondary education and increase its share inenrollments
         to 13.2 percent.

28 The effects o f the policies that improvements efficiency and utilization are cumulative.
29 The target number o f teachers hours used inthe simulations was 25. Additional simulations indicate
    that the deficit will be reducedby a substantial margin ifteaching hours were increased to 27.


                                                     184

     0   Improve the management o f teachers by increasingthe effective class size to 45.
         This can be achieved through the use o fmulti-shiftsandmandatingthe minimum
         class size o f 30 students.

     0   Improve the utilization o f teacher time by employing more polyvalent teachers
          andtraining them to teach three or more subjects. The mandatory number o f
         hours worked by teachers worked eachweek should increase to 25.

18.      A decline inprimaryschool-age population after 2010 has the potential to create
additional flexibility for the Government to transfer resources from primaryto secondary
education. Infact, there i s a potential to reduce the number o f teachers by about 15000 in
2015 ifthe policy to increase the average student-teacher ratio to 45 inprimaryeducation
i s implementedby the Government.

pre-university education intotal Government budget i s kept constant, it is evident from
the above discussion that the available resources will be adequate to cover projected
expenditure over the plan period o f the KESSP (2006-2010). The primary education
sector will be sustainable and attain a completion rate o f 100percent on its way to reach
other targets o f the FTIby 2015. Secondary education goal o f 70 percent transition rate
(by2008), on the other hand, is also sustainable butonly under a stringent set o fpolicies
that have to be implementedwithin the next two years. Giventhe projecteddecline in

20.      To recapitulate, ifthe present regime o fbudgetaryallocations and internal
efficiency continue, the education systemwill face difficulties with regards to fiscal
sustainability after 2010. An improvement inthe efficiency through a reduction in
repetition rate will bringsome relief, but the secondary education sub sector will continue
to be unsustainable. The simulations indicate that the introduction o fpolyvalent teachers
scenario bring about the largest gains inefficiency, and reduce the deficit significantly in
secondary education. The gains inefficiency, utilization andprivate sector participation
are sufficient to eliminate the deficit, and an injection of small amount o f additional
resources inconjunction with the transfer o f resources from primary to the secondary will
be sufficient to assure the financial sustainability o fthe pre-university education system
after 2010.

21.      Inconclusion, the above scenarios for the two education sub sectors, serve to
illustrate that the Government should be cautious inproceedingwith its expansion o f
secondary education. These are the best-case scenarios, inwhich all objectives outlined
inthe KESSP, andimprovementsinthe efficiency ofservice deliveryparameters have
been achieved. Ifsimilar gains prove to be difficult to replicate inthe real world,
additional savings will have to be found elsewhere inthe education sector. These
simulations account for additional expenditures requiredfor quality improvements, such
as training of teachers, grants to schools andmany other inputs (within the KESSP
program).


                                              187

22.     The FTIobjective o f 100percent completion rate, however, canbe attained ina
fiscally sustainable programby 2010 andthe primary education sector will be on its way
to reach the other objectives by 2015. Because o f the increase inthe number ofprimary
school students after the introduction of free education in2003, it may face the challenge
o f maintaining that rate after that bulge of students' passesthrough the primary education
cycle in2010.
